5. **The Feminine and Symbolic Fragility** :Rose’s character can be analyzed through Lacan’s concept of the **feminine**  as the Other that is excluded from the phallic order. Rose represents an intrusion into Phil’s carefully constructed masculine world. Her very presence destabilizes Phil’s symbolic authority, and his campaign to drive her to alcoholism can be seen as an attempt to neutralize this threat.
Rose’s fragility and emotional vulnerability contrast sharply with Phil’s rigid masculinity, but both characters are ultimately trapped within the same symbolic structure. Rose, too, is marginalized and pushed to the edges of the Symbolic Order, as she becomes increasingly alienated from George and consumed by her own sense of failure.

#### Conclusion: 
In *The Power of the Dog*, Lacanian psychoanalysis helps to unravel the complex interplay between repression, desire, and power. Phil Burbank’s character is defined by his entrapment in the **Imaginary** —his attachment to an idealized image of masculinity—and his failure to integrate his repressed desires into the **Symbolic Order** . His unresolved trauma and denial of the **Real**  lead to his downfall, while his jouissance drives him toward self-destruction. Through this lens, the film explores the devastating consequences of repression and the fragile nature of identity when it is built on a foundation of denial and unresolved desire.

## Phatom Thread movie

In analyzing *Phantom Thread* (2017) through the lens of Lacanian psychoanalysis, we can explore themes of power, control, desire, and the dynamics of love within the Symbolic, Imaginary, and Real realms. The film, directed by Paul Thomas Anderson, follows the obsessive and controlling couture designer Reynolds Woodcock and his evolving relationship with Alma, a young woman who becomes both his muse and his emotional equal.1. **Reynolds and the Imaginary** :Reynolds exists within the **Imaginary Order** , where he seeks to control and perfect the world around him, particularly through his art of dressmaking. He identifies himself with his craft, meticulously designing clothes that act as extensions of his self-image and his need for mastery over others, particularly women. His obsession with precision and beauty reflects a deeper desire to control his environment, reducing any unpredictability or intrusion from external forces that might disrupt his sense of self.The dresses Reynolds creates are like mirrors that reflect his vision of ideal femininity. In Lacanian terms, this is a way of engaging with the **Imaginary** : Reynolds creates a fantasy world where he can dictate how women should look and behave, molding their identities according to his desires. This need for control, however, is constantly threatened by the intrusion of the **Real** —the unpredictable elements of life that cannot be controlled or symbolized.2. **Alma and the Real** :Alma, at first, appears to be merely an object of Reynolds’ desire—another woman to be shaped and controlled within his world. However, she gradually becomes an embodiment of the **Real**  for Reynolds, disrupting the perfectly ordered life he has constructed. Alma does not conform to his expectations and begins to assert her own desires and autonomy, challenging Reynolds' control over their relationship.Lacan's concept of the **Real**  refers to what lies beyond language and the symbolic order—those raw, unmediated experiences that cannot be fully controlled or understood. Alma introduces Reynolds to the uncontrollable aspects of human relationships, such as vulnerability, dependency, and the need for emotional intimacy. Her decision to use poisonous mushrooms to make him physically ill is a literal confrontation with the **Real** , as it forces Reynolds to relinquish his control and rely on her for care, disrupting his carefully curated world.3. **Desire and the Symbolic Order** :In Lacanian theory, **desire**  is always mediated by the **Other** , and in *Phantom Thread*, Reynolds' desire is initially shaped by the demands of the **Symbolic Order** —his role as a successful designer, his reputation, and the expectations placed on him by society. His need to maintain control over his work and his relationships is part of his adherence to the Symbolic structures that define his identity.
Alma, however, disrupts this dynamic by asserting her own desires. Her refusal to be simply another beautiful object in Reynolds’ life challenges the Symbolic structures that have governed their relationship. Alma’s assertion of agency ultimately forces Reynolds to confront his own repressed desires—his need for love, dependence, and vulnerability, which he had previously denied in order to maintain his sense of control.
4. **Jouissance and Repetition** :Lacan’s concept of **jouissance**  refers to a form of excessive pleasure that is intertwined with pain and transgression. Reynolds’ relationship with Alma becomes an arena of jouissance, as their dynamic involves repeated cycles of domination, illness, and care. Alma’s poisoning of Reynolds can be seen as a way of both asserting control over him and allowing him to experience a kind of jouissance—the pleasure of surrendering his rigid control over his life.
Reynolds comes to find a strange enjoyment in the moments when Alma nurses him back to health after poisoning him. This cycle of sickness and recovery creates a new form of intimacy between them, one that is based on mutual dependency and the transgression of societal norms. Their relationship evolves into a kind of symbiotic bond that thrives on this interplay of power, surrender, and care.
5. **The Name-of-the-Father and the Role of Authority** :Lacan’s concept of the **Name-of-the-Father**  refers to the symbolic law or authority that structures desire and social relationships. In *Phantom Thread*, Reynolds represents the authoritative figure in his relationships—he is the master designer, the one who dictates the terms of his interactions with women. However, Alma’s challenge to his authority introduces a crisis in this symbolic order.Alma’s actions force Reynolds to reconsider his role and authority, leading to a reconfiguration of the **Symbolic**  structure of their relationship. By the end of the film, Reynolds voluntarily enters into a new dynamic where his authority is no longer absolute, and he accepts Alma’s role as an equal partner in their strange but functional relationship. This reflects a shift in the **Symbolic Order** —Reynolds acknowledges that he can no longer be the sole authority in his life, and he makes room for Alma’s influence and desires.
#### Conclusion: 
Through a Lacanian lens, *Phantom Thread* can be seen as a film about the breakdown of control and the confrontation with repressed desires. Reynolds' obsessive need to control both his art and his relationships is challenged by Alma, who represents the **Real** —the uncontrollable and unpredictable aspects of human existence. Their relationship, marked by cycles of domination and surrender, reflects a deeper exploration of desire, jouissance, and the restructuring of authority. In the end, the film shows how the rigid structures of the **Symbolic Order**  can be disrupted by the intrusion of the **Real** , leading to a new understanding of intimacy, dependence, and love.

## A Ghost Story, movie

In *A Ghost Story* (2017), directed by David Lowery, the film explores themes of time, grief, memory, and the persistence of existence through the minimalist tale of a man (Casey Affleck) who dies and returns as a ghost. The narrative, which spans vast stretches of time, focuses on the ghost’s haunting presence in his former home, observing the lives of those who inhabit it and the inevitable passage of time.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Symbolic and the Void of Identity** :
From a Lacanian perspective, the ghost embodies the **void of identity** . Upon death, the man no longer occupies a place within the **Symbolic Order** , which structures identity and social relationships. The white-sheeted ghost, stripped of its former human characteristics, becomes an anonymous figure, reflecting the absence of a signifier in the Symbolic Order that gives him meaning. He no longer functions within the structure of language, social norms, or personal relationships.Lacan’s notion of the **Name-of-the-Father** , which anchors a person in the Symbolic Order by providing a place in society and language, is absent for the ghost. As a result, he exists outside the realms of communication, meaning, and relationships, and his endless wandering represents a search for a return to some form of symbolic identity, but he is unable to re-enter this system.
 
2. **The Real and the Experience of Time** :
The ghost's experience of time can be related to Lacan’s concept of the **Real** —the aspect of existence that cannot be symbolized, understood, or controlled by language. As the ghost witnesses time pass in a non-linear way, he is confronted with the raw and unmediated passage of life and history, which is impossible to fully comprehend. This time is cyclical and endless, reflecting the Real’s disruptive nature—time becomes an inescapable reality that the ghost cannot grasp or escape.The ghost’s observation of different inhabitants of the house, including future developments far beyond his original life, reflects how the **Real**  confronts individuals with forces they cannot control or symbolize, creating a sense of trauma or existential crisis.
 
3. **Desire and Jouissance** :
The ghost’s attachment to the house and his inability to let go of his previous life may be understood through Lacan’s notion of **desire** . His longing to stay in the house, close to his wife (Rooney Mara), represents a clinging to lost desire, a desperate attempt to re-enter the Symbolic Order and regain what has been lost. Yet, this desire can never be fulfilled—he cannot communicate with the living, nor can he move forward in any meaningful way.The **jouissance**  (excessive pleasure tied to suffering) of the ghost’s existence lies in his inability to reconcile his desires. His lingering in the house is both a source of torment and a form of attachment to his lost life, making his existence a cycle of unfulfilled desires and suffering. He experiences time without meaning, a form of excessive existence without resolution.
 
4. **The Imaginary and the Persistence of Memory** :
The ghost’s obsession with a note left behind by his wife functions within the **Imaginary**  realm, where he projects his need for closure or meaning onto this small object. The note, which he cannot access for much of the film, becomes a symbol of what he has lost and what he yearns for—a remnant of the past that allows him to retain a sense of connection with his previous life.Lacan's concept of the **objet petit a** , or the object of desire that perpetually escapes our grasp, can be seen in the ghost’s fixation on the note. It symbolizes his unresolved longing for meaning and connection, but it also represents the unattainable, as he cannot truly interact with the world of the living.

#### Conclusion: 
 
- A Ghost Story* can be seen as an exploration of Lacanian concepts such as the breakdown of identity in the absence of the **Symbolic Order** , the confrontation with the **Real**  through the passage of time, and the ghost’s unresolved desires. His haunting presence is an embodiment of a subject trapped between life and death, meaning and meaninglessness, endlessly searching for closure that cannot be symbolized or achieved. The film beautifully encapsulates the tension between existence and non-existence, desire and loss, within a minimalist yet profound narrative.

## Portrait of a Lady on Fire, movie

*Portrait of a Lady on Fire* (2019), directed by Céline Sciamma, offers a rich text for Lacanian psychoanalytic analysis, particularly in its exploration of desire, the gaze, and the formation of subjectivity. The film follows the slow-burning romance between Marianne, a painter, and Héloïse, a woman she has been hired to paint in secret. It presents themes of desire, forbidden love, and artistic creation, all of which lend themselves to deeper psychoanalytic inquiry.1. **The Gaze and Desire** :In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the concept of the **gaze**  is central to understanding desire and subject formation. Throughout *Portrait of a Lady on Fire*, the act of looking—both from the artist and the subject—creates a complex dynamic of desire. Marianne’s role as the painter positions her as the one who looks, but her growing affection for Héloïse turns the gaze into an act of desire. Marianne’s desire is not only for Héloïse as a person but also for the artistic creation that captures her.Lacan’s **objet petit a** , the unattainable object of desire, is embodied in the portrait itself. The painting represents an attempt to capture Héloïse, but it remains incomplete without the emotional and intimate connection that arises between them. The painting becomes a medium through which desire is expressed but never fully realized, as the love between Marianne and Héloïse is fleeting and constrained by external circumstances.2. **The Symbolic and Forbidden Love** :The film’s exploration of forbidden love fits within Lacan’s concept of the **Symbolic Order** , the realm of societal rules, language, and law. Marianne and Héloïse’s love exists outside the boundaries of the Symbolic Order, as it defies the conventional roles and expectations of women in their society. Héloïse is meant to be married off, and Marianne is merely the artist hired to paint her. The love between the two women, while deeply felt, remains outside the bounds of what society can acknowledge or support.The **Name-of-the-Father** , which represents authority and the societal law, looms over their relationship, especially through the figure of Héloïse’s unseen fiancé, who symbolizes the social structures that will ultimately separate them. The absence of men in the film creates a space where the women’s desires can exist momentarily without constraint, but the eventual return to reality highlights the inescapability of the Symbolic Order.3. **The Imaginary and the Formation of Identity** :Lacan’s **Imaginary**  is the realm where the subject forms a sense of self through the reflection of others. In *Portrait of a Lady on Fire*, the painting process serves as a mirror through which both Marianne and Héloïse form their identities. Marianne, as the artist, is constantly reflecting on her ability to capture Héloïse’s image, while Héloïse, as the subject, grapples with how she is seen by both Marianne and the world.
The film subtly plays with the notion of identity being constructed through the gaze of the other. Héloïse’s reluctance to be painted at first suggests a resistance to being defined or fixed in an image. However, as the relationship between the two women deepens, the act of painting becomes a mutual creation of identity, where both women come to understand themselves and each other more fully.
4. **The Real and the Tragic End** :In Lacanian terms, the **Real**  represents the aspect of existence that cannot be fully symbolized or integrated into the Imaginary or Symbolic. In *Portrait of a Lady on Fire*, the inescapable separation between Marianne and Héloïse represents the intrusion of the Real into their idyllic love. Despite their deep emotional connection, societal norms and expectations (the Symbolic) will eventually force them apart, and this reality is something neither of them can overcome.
The final scenes of the film, particularly the opera scene where Marianne sees Héloïse from afar, reflect the painful presence of the Real. The memory of their love continues to haunt Marianne, but it exists as a lost possibility—something that can never be fully recaptured or resolved within the constraints of their lives. The film leaves the audience with this lingering sense of loss and the unresolvable nature of desire, a hallmark of Lacanian thought.

#### Conclusion: 
*Portrait of a Lady on Fire* provides a powerful exploration of Lacanian concepts such as the gaze, desire, the Symbolic Order, and the Real. The film’s delicate portrayal of love and loss between Marianne and Héloïse reflects the tensions between societal constraints and personal desire, the attempt to capture the unattainable, and the tragic reality that some desires can never be fully realized. The film’s minimalist style and focus on the act of looking serve to heighten its exploration of these psychoanalytic themes, making it a deeply resonant and thought-provoking work.

## The fabelmans

*The Fabelmans* (2022), directed by Steven Spielberg, is a semi-autobiographical film that reflects the filmmaker’s own childhood and his journey toward becoming one of the most influential directors in cinema. Set in the 1950s and 1960s, the movie focuses on young Sammy Fabelman, a character based on Spielberg, as he discovers his love for filmmaking amidst the complexities of his family dynamics.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Gaze and the Act of Filmmaking** :
In Lacanian theory, the **gaze**  is central to subject formation and the experience of desire. Sammy’s fascination with filmmaking can be understood through the Lacanian concept of the gaze. As a filmmaker, Sammy occupies the position of the one who watches, but in doing so, he also confronts the gaze of others. His films become a way for him to control and interpret the world around him, allowing him to craft a version of reality where he is both the observer and the creator.Sammy's first encounter with filmmaking—when he sees his first movie, *The Greatest Show on Earth*—marks the beginning of his obsession with recreating what he has seen. This desire to recreate and capture the world on film can be seen as his attempt to master the gaze, to impose his own vision of the world and to process the trauma and complexities of his family life through the lens of the camera.
 
2. **The Symbolic and Family Dynamics** :
Lacan’s **Symbolic Order**  refers to the structures of language, culture, and societal rules that organize human relationships. In *The Fabelmans*, Sammy’s family is characterized by the tensions between his artistic mother, Mitzi, and his more pragmatic father, Burt. These two figures represent different aspects of the Symbolic Order—Mitzi embodies creativity, spontaneity, and emotional expression, while Burt represents rationality, order, and adherence to social norms.
Sammy’s filmmaking is caught between these two worlds. His mother encourages his artistic pursuits, while his father views them as a hobby rather than a serious career path. This conflict mirrors Sammy’s internal struggle between fulfilling his artistic desires and conforming to societal expectations. The act of filmmaking becomes Sammy’s way of navigating the Symbolic Order, as he attempts to reconcile the emotional and intellectual aspects of his identity.
 
3. **The Real and the Revelation of Truth** :
In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the **Real**  is the raw, unmediated aspect of existence that cannot be fully symbolized or understood through language. In *The Fabelmans*, the Real intrudes into Sammy’s life when he discovers his mother’s affair with his father’s best friend, Bennie. This discovery, made through his editing of home movie footage, shatters the illusion of his family’s stability and forces Sammy to confront a painful truth that had been hidden.
The act of filming and editing allows Sammy to access the Real, as his camera captures the unspoken and unconscious dynamics within his family. The moment of revelation is traumatic for Sammy, as it disrupts the symbolic structures that had previously defined his understanding of his family. This confrontation with the Real forces him to reevaluate his relationships and his own identity as a filmmaker.
 
4. **Desire and the Objet Petit a** :
Sammy’s passion for filmmaking can also be understood through Lacan’s concept of **desire**  and the **objet petit a** —the unattainable object of desire that drives human actions. Sammy’s desire to capture the world on film is motivated by a deeper, unconscious desire to understand and control the complexities of his emotions and relationships. His camera becomes the objet petit a, a tool through which he seeks to fulfill his desire for mastery and understanding, but which ultimately eludes him.
The camera allows Sammy to explore his desires, but it also reveals the limits of what can be captured or understood. No matter how much he films, Sammy can never fully capture the emotional truth of his experiences, as there is always something beyond the frame—something in the Real that cannot be symbolized or represented.

#### Conclusion: 
*The Fabelmans* is a deeply personal film that explores the intersections of desire, family dynamics, and artistic creation through a Lacanian lens. Sammy’s journey as a filmmaker reflects his attempts to navigate the tensions between the Imaginary, the Symbolic, and the Real, as he uses filmmaking as a way to process his desires and confront the painful truths of his family life. The film ultimately suggests that art, while a powerful tool for understanding and expression, can never fully capture the complexities of human experience, leaving certain aspects of the Real forever beyond our grasp.

## Marriage story

In *Marriage Story* (2019), directed by Noah Baumbach, the film centers on the emotional breakdown of a marriage between Charlie (Adam Driver) and Nicole (Scarlett Johansson), capturing their journey through divorce. The film portrays the deep emotional struggles of separation, while also reflecting on the nuances of love, conflict, and family dynamics.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Fall of the Ideal** :
Lacan’s concept of **desire**  is central to *Marriage Story*. Both Charlie and Nicole are shown to have desires that initially aligned during their marriage but began to diverge over time. Charlie’s desire is rooted in his career and his need for control and recognition as a successful theater director, while Nicole’s desire is for personal autonomy and recognition of her own identity, which she feels has been overshadowed by her role as Charlie’s wife.Lacanian desire is always driven by a sense of **lack** , and for Nicole, this lack becomes unbearable as she begins to feel like an object in Charlie’s world rather than a subject with her own desires and ambitions. The breakdown of their relationship reflects the impossibility of fully satisfying one’s desires within a partnership, as each partner’s desires inevitably diverge.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Legal Structures** :
Lacan’s **Symbolic Order** , representing societal laws, language, and norms, plays a major role in the film, especially through the legal system that structures the divorce process. The legal proceedings, while meant to be impartial and objective, become a battlefield where each party's needs and desires are distorted by the **symbolic structures**  that aim to mediate their separation. Lawyers, such as Nora (Laura Dern) and Jay (Ray Liotta), are the enforcers of the Symbolic Order, shaping how Charlie and Nicole articulate their grievances and justify their actions.The film critiques how the legal system forces the couple to engage in combative language that exacerbates their conflict rather than resolving it. Lacan’s concept of the **Name-of-the-Father**  comes into play here, as the law (the Father’s name) enforces boundaries and rules that restrict both parties’ desires, driving the conflict further.
 
3. **The Gaze and Misrecognition** :
In Lacan’s theory, the **gaze**  represents the way one is looked at and how individuals internalize this gaze, forming their identity in response to it. Both Charlie and Nicole experience moments of **misrecognition** —the failure to be recognized by the other in a way that reflects their true selves. Charlie, for instance, sees himself as a devoted father and husband, but Nicole’s perspective reveals that he has been emotionally distant and controlling. Conversely, Nicole’s move toward independence is seen by Charlie as selfishness, while she views it as a necessary step toward reclaiming her identity.The scenes in which they attempt to articulate their frustrations and pain reflect this misrecognition—each is unable to fully understand or validate the other’s experience. This dynamic reflects Lacan’s **Imaginary**  register, where the ego is built around images and perceptions of self that are always incomplete or distorted.
 
4. **The Real and Emotional Breakdown** :
The confrontation with the **Real** —that which cannot be fully symbolized or explained in language—occurs in the most emotionally charged moments of the film. One of the key scenes where this happens is during the heated argument between Charlie and Nicole, where their carefully constructed personas break down, revealing raw, unmediated emotions. Charlie’s rage and Nicole’s despair reflect their encounter with the **Real** , the traumatic core of their failing relationship that cannot be easily resolved through rational discourse or legal proceedings.
This scene shows how both characters are grappling with the Real of their emotional pain—something that exists beyond words and societal frameworks. The Real is what haunts them, manifesting in their inability to fully process their loss or understand their own role in the dissolution of their marriage.

#### Conclusion: 
*Marriage Story* offers a complex exploration of Lacanian themes, including the nature of desire, the distortions of the Symbolic Order, and the impossibility of fully recognizing or being recognized by the other. The film presents the breakdown of a marriage as a reflection of deeper psychoanalytic tensions between identity, desire, and societal norms. The conflict between Charlie and Nicole exemplifies how even the most intimate relationships are shaped by unconscious forces and external structures that both enable and constrain their desires.

## Man in an orange shirt, Lacanian analysis

*Man in an Orange Shirt* (2017), part of the BBC's "Gay Britannia" season, tells two intergenerational stories about forbidden love between men. The first story, set during WWII, follows Michael Berryman, a soldier who falls in love with a fellow soldier, Thomas. The second part, set in the present day, focuses on Adam, Michael’s grandson, as he navigates his own complicated feelings around intimacy and love. Lacanian psychoanalytic concepts are particularly useful in analyzing the tensions between desire, societal norms, and identity in both time periods.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Real:** 
In both Michael’s and Adam’s stories, desire is central, but it is shaped and constrained by the **Symbolic Order**  (society’s laws and norms). Lacan’s **desire**  refers to something that remains elusive and unattainable, and in this film, desire is structured around the impossibility of fully expressing or fulfilling it within a heteronormative society. Michael’s intense but forbidden love for Thomas is a clear manifestation of this. Their desire exists outside the realm of what society allows, and their inability to fully integrate this love into their lives speaks to the Lacanian **Real** —that which cannot be fully symbolized or assimilated into language or society.
In Adam’s case, although he lives in a more accepting time, he still grapples with an inner conflict around intimacy, trust, and love. His relationships reflect the same tension between his desire and the Real—he can never fully express or achieve what he wants because the trauma and repression of previous generations still haunt him.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Repression:** 
Michael's marriage to Flora represents an entry into the **Symbolic Order** , where he conforms to the social expectation of a heterosexual relationship. The marriage, though, is marked by repression—Michael cannot express his true desires, and Flora, although aware of his secret, is trapped within the same societal structure. The lack of true emotional and physical intimacy in their relationship is indicative of the **Name-of-the-Father** , Lacan’s term for the law and societal order that governs and limits desire. Michael’s inability to live openly with his love for Thomas speaks to the way the Symbolic Order represses queer desire.For Adam, the Symbolic Order has shifted, and society is more accepting of same-sex relationships. However, the internalized repression passed down from Michael’s generation continues to affect Adam, demonstrating how the **unconscious**  is structured by the Symbolic. His inability to connect fully with his lovers reflects a deeper, unresolved tension between societal acceptance and personal psychological barriers.
 
3. **The Gaze and Identity:** 
Lacan’s concept of the **gaze** —the way in which individuals are seen and perceive themselves in relation to others—is crucial in both stories. Michael’s fear of being exposed and judged by society for his relationship with Thomas represents the anxiety of the gaze. His life is shaped by the pressure of conforming to societal norms and the fear of being recognized as something other than what society deems acceptable. This gaze forces him to hide his true identity and live a life of repression.
Adam, although not under the same societal pressures, still feels the weight of his family's history and society’s expectations. His inability to maintain a stable relationship with Steve, and his reluctance to commit emotionally, reflects his internalized gaze—he sees himself through the lens of inherited shame and unresolved trauma from his grandfather’s generation.
 
4. **The Imaginary and the Fantasy of Love:** 
Both Michael and Adam struggle with the **Imaginary** —Lacan’s realm of images and fantasies through which individuals form their identities and experience desire. Michael’s relationship with Thomas is idealized, existing more in the realm of fantasy than reality because it cannot be fully integrated into his life. The photograph of the two men together in the painting (the "man in an orange shirt") becomes a symbol of that fantasy, an image of a love that exists only in secret and in memory.Adam, on the other hand, idealizes the idea of love but struggles to bring it into reality. His encounters with other men, while physically intimate, lack the emotional depth he craves. The gap between the fantasy of love and the reality of his relationships mirrors Lacan’s notion of the **objet petit a** —the unattainable object of desire that can never be fully grasped.

#### Conclusion: 
*Man in an Orange Shirt* is a powerful exploration of desire, repression, and the effects of societal norms on identity across generations. Through a Lacanian lens, the film highlights the ways in which queer desire is structured by the Symbolic Order, how the gaze shapes identity, and how the unattainable nature of desire leaves characters struggling to reconcile their internal desires with external realities. The film underscores the lasting psychological impact of repression and the complex ways in which desire and identity are negotiated within different cultural and temporal contexts.

## Paterson, movie

*Paterson* (2016), directed by Jim Jarmusch, is a contemplative film about a week in the life of Paterson (Adam Driver), a bus driver in Paterson, New Jersey, who also writes poetry. The film presents an understated yet profound look at the rhythm of daily life, creativity, and the search for meaning in simplicity.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and the Ordinary** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Real**  refers to what lies beyond the symbolic and imaginary structures that give meaning to our lives. *Paterson* is a film that focuses on the Real of everyday life—repetitive, mundane activities that are not overtly dramatic or extraordinary. Paterson’s life is defined by routine: driving the bus, walking his dog, and writing poetry in his notebook. Yet, in this ordinariness, Paterson’s poetry gives a glimpse of the Real—a kind of raw, immediate engagement with the world that escapes the structured narratives of ambition or conventional success.
The poems, often simple observations about his environment or small moments in his day, reflect his ability to access the Real in the midst of the mundane. The fact that his poems are not shared or published adds to this sense of intimacy with the Real—his creative process is not about external recognition but about personal connection with life’s subtleties.
 
2. **The Imaginary and the Creative Self** :
The **Imaginary**  in Lacanian theory deals with the formation of the ego through images and mirrors. Paterson’s quiet life with his girlfriend Laura (Golshifteh Farahani) reflects a balance between the Imaginary and the Real. Laura is full of creative ideas, constantly redecorating their home, learning new skills, and dreaming of future success. Her boundless creativity can be seen as her engagement with the Imaginary—a space where identity and dreams are fluid and open to change.
Paterson, by contrast, remains more rooted in his routine and seems to resist this fluidity. His poetry is an extension of his inner world, shaped by observation rather than transformation. Laura’s constant reimagining of her surroundings contrasts with Paterson’s quiet, consistent self, suggesting two different ways of relating to the Imaginary: Laura reshapes it actively, while Paterson reflects on it through his poems.
 
3. **The Symbolic Order and Language** :
Lacan’s **Symbolic Order**  is the realm of language, culture, and societal structures. Paterson’s relationship with language is central to the film, particularly in how he uses words to construct meaning in an unremarkable life. His poetry is his way of engaging with the Symbolic Order, yet it remains outside the typical frameworks of fame or publication. The poems, jotted down in a private notebook, show how Paterson is content to create without needing the validation of the larger societal Symbolic structure.
Furthermore, the film frequently shows Paterson listening to fragments of conversations on the bus. These snippets of dialogue highlight the ways people use language to create their own symbolic worlds, each person existing within their own subjective universe. Paterson, the quiet observer, takes these moments in, but remains somewhat detached from the dominant Symbolic Order, as his poetry allows him to create a personal space outside of societal pressures.
 
4. **The Lack and Desire** :
Lacanian **lack**  is fundamental to desire—it is the absence or incompleteness that drives human actions. Paterson’s life, while tranquil, is marked by a certain lack. He is not overtly ambitious, and while he loves poetry, he does not push for publication or recognition. This lack of external ambition reflects a deeper contentment with the space of desire rather than fulfillment. He is, in a way, comfortable with his lack—his poetry is not about achieving something, but rather about expressing what remains incomplete or fleeting.
In contrast, Laura embodies more visible forms of desire. She dreams of success, whether in baking or learning the guitar, and she is constantly striving to transform her environment. This dynamic between the two shows how Paterson engages with a more subtle, internal sense of lack, whereas Laura’s desires are more external and connected to societal symbols of success.

#### Conclusion: 
In *Paterson*, Lacanian concepts of the Real, the Imaginary, and the Symbolic Order are intricately woven into the fabric of the film’s narrative. Paterson’s poetry becomes a window into his engagement with the Real—the unspoken, quiet depths of everyday life—while his relationship with Laura and the external world reflects different ways of engaging with the Imaginary and Symbolic. The film offers a meditation on the nature of desire, creativity, and identity, revealing how meaning can be found in the repetition and stillness of ordinary life.

## Ladybird

*Lady Bird* (2017), directed by Greta Gerwig, is a coming-of-age film that follows Christine "Lady Bird" McPherson (Saoirse Ronan), a high school senior in Sacramento, as she navigates the complexities of adolescence, relationships, and her desire to find her own identity. The film’s exploration of familial tension, self-discovery, and class issues makes it a rich subject for Lacanian psychoanalysis, especially in relation to concepts of desire, identity, and the role of the Other.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Symbolic Order** :
In Lacanian theory, **desire**  is formed through the Subject’s entry into the **Symbolic Order** —the world of language, social structures, and norms that shape identity and behavior. Lady Bird’s desire to leave Sacramento and attend college on the East Coast reflects her need to break free from the confines of the Symbolic structures around her—her family, her Catholic school, and her socioeconomic status. She feels stifled by these external constraints and is searching for an identity that exists outside of the roles imposed on her.
Lady Bird’s rebelliousness and yearning for change highlight her struggle with this Symbolic Order. Her renaming of herself (from Christine to Lady Bird) is an attempt to assert her own identity, separate from what her mother and society expect of her. This desire for autonomy and reinvention reflects Lacan’s notion that the Subject is always searching for something beyond what the Symbolic can offer.
 
2. **The Imaginary and the Relationship with the Mother** :
Lacan’s **Imaginary**  refers to the realm of images and ego-formation, particularly in relation to the "mirror stage," where the Subject forms an identity based on how they perceive themselves in relation to others. The tension between Lady Bird and her mother, Marion (Laurie Metcalf), is a central dynamic in the film and can be analyzed through the lens of the Imaginary.Marion’s often critical and controlling behavior toward Lady Bird can be seen as a reflection of the **misrecognition**  that occurs in the Imaginary. Lady Bird seeks her mother’s approval but is also deeply frustrated by the way her mother perceives her. Marion, in turn, sees Lady Bird through the lens of her own anxieties about money, responsibility, and what it means to be successful. The two are locked in a cycle of projection and misunderstanding, typical of relationships where the Imaginary dominates. Lady Bird’s struggle to see herself as an independent, valued person apart from her mother’s judgments represents her attempt to step out of this Imaginary relationship.
 
3. **The Name-of-the-Father and Social Mobility** :
The **Name-of-the-Father** , in Lacanian terms, refers to the law and authority that structure the Subject’s entry into society. In *Lady Bird*, the notion of authority and societal expectations is frequently tied to themes of class and social mobility. Lady Bird’s father, Larry (Tracy Letts), is unemployed, and her family struggles with financial issues. This socioeconomic tension creates a backdrop for Lady Bird’s desire to escape her current life and the limitations it imposes.Lady Bird’s longing to attend a prestigious East Coast college, despite her family’s financial situation, symbolizes her attempt to bypass the limitations set by the Symbolic Order of her social class. She seeks the validation of institutions and a lifestyle that she perceives as representing success and freedom. This desire to transcend her socioeconomic status can be seen as a rejection of the **paternal law**  that governs her life, a defiance of the limits imposed by her family’s circumstances.
 
4. **The Role of the Other** :
Lacan’s concept of the **Other**  represents the social world, language, and structures that shape our subjectivity. Throughout the film, Lady Bird’s relationships with others—her friends, love interests, and teachers—serve as mirrors that reflect her search for identity. Her relationship with Danny (Lucas Hedges) and later with Kyle (Timothée Chalamet) highlights her attempts to find validation and recognition outside of herself, through the gaze of others.
However, each relationship leaves her unsatisfied, as the Other can never fully provide the recognition or fulfillment she seeks. Kyle, in particular, represents an illusory ideal of rebellious, intellectual freedom, but Lady Bird quickly realizes that this persona is hollow and disconnected from the emotional intimacy she truly craves. This mirrors Lacan’s idea that the Subject is always in search of something beyond the Other’s recognition, as desire is never fully satisfied by external validation.

#### Conclusion: 
*Lady Bird* presents a rich tapestry of relationships and desires that can be understood through Lacanian psychoanalysis. Lady Bird’s struggle to find her own identity outside of the roles imposed on her by family, society, and socioeconomic structures reflects the Lacanian notion of desire as rooted in lack and the tension between the Symbolic and Imaginary. Her relationship with her mother, in particular, is a key site of conflict, revealing how desire, identity, and self-worth are formed in relation to the Other, and how the pursuit of independence is fraught with the challenges of reconciling internal desires with external expectations.

## Mala Noche

*Mala Noche* (1986), directed by Gus Van Sant, is an influential queer film and marks his feature debut. The story is based on the autobiographical novel by Walt Curtis and explores themes of unrequited love, desire, and cultural barriers. The film follows Walt, a gay man working in a liquor store in Portland, Oregon, who becomes infatuated with Johnny, a young undocumented Mexican immigrant. The power dynamics of race, class, and sexuality are central to the film's exploration of desire and longing.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Unattainable Object** :
One of the key themes in *Mala Noche* is unrequited desire, which aligns with Lacan’s theory of **desire**  and the **objet petit a** —the unattainable object of desire that is central to the formation of subjectivity. Walt’s infatuation with Johnny represents a kind of impossible longing, as Johnny does not reciprocate his feelings. Johnny’s elusiveness symbolizes the unattainable object in Lacanian terms, with Walt’s desire remaining unfulfilled, driving the narrative tension. Johnny is not just a person for Walt but an idealized fantasy, embodying what Lacan describes as the gap between desire and satisfaction.
 
2. **The Role of the Other** :
In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the **Other**  represents the external world of language, culture, and societal structures. In *Mala Noche*, the cultural and linguistic differences between Walt and Johnny emphasize the role of the Other in shaping desire. Walt's inability to fully connect with Johnny is partly due to the language barrier and their different social backgrounds. Johnny’s status as an undocumented immigrant places him within the realm of the Other, as he is marginalized within the social order. For Walt, Johnny represents both a desirable Other and an impossible ideal that is out of reach.
The film also portrays the dynamic between the dominant and marginalized cultures, where Walt’s whiteness and economic stability contrast with Johnny’s precarious position as an immigrant. This cultural divide reinforces the impossibility of their connection, creating a tension between Walt's desire and the barriers imposed by the symbolic order of society.
 
3. **The Imaginary and Identity Formation** :
Lacan’s concept of the **Imaginary**  involves the construction of identity through images, especially during the **mirror stage**  when a child forms a sense of self by recognizing their reflection. In *Mala Noche*, Walt’s pursuit of Johnny is intertwined with his self-perception and the way he imagines himself in relation to Johnny. Walt’s identity is shaped by his role as an outsider seeking connection with someone from a different world, and this dynamic mirrors the Imaginary stage where one’s self-image is formed in relation to others.
Walt’s fixation on Johnny can be seen as an attempt to affirm his own identity through the object of his desire. His efforts to bridge the gap between them—by offering Johnny money, trying to form a bond—reflect his attempts to reconcile the split between his internal desires and external reality. However, this ultimately leads to frustration, as Johnny remains elusive and uninterested in reciprocating Walt’s advances.
 
4. **Power Dynamics and the Symbolic Order** :
The film also delves into the power dynamics between Walt and Johnny, highlighting issues of class, race, and sexuality. Walt’s economic privilege contrasts with Johnny’s vulnerability as an undocumented immigrant, creating a tension within their interactions. Lacan’s concept of the **Name-of-the-Father** , which represents the law and authority of the symbolic order, is relevant here as Walt’s desire is constrained by societal structures. He occupies a position of power in relation to Johnny, but this power does not allow him to fully possess what he desires. Instead, the symbolic structures of society, such as immigration status and class, prevent Walt from achieving his fantasy of a romantic connection.
Additionally, the law is always present in the background of the narrative, whether through the threat of deportation for Johnny or the legal barriers that prevent Walt from fully engaging with his desire. The film critiques these structures, showing how they limit freedom and create divisions between people based on race, class, and nationality.

#### Conclusion: 
*Mala Noche* presents a rich text for Lacanian analysis, especially in its portrayal of unfulfilled desire, the role of the Other, and the power dynamics that shape relationships. Walt’s pursuit of Johnny is marked by the impossibility of fulfilling his desires within the constraints of the symbolic order, reflecting the Lacanian idea that desire is always structured around a fundamental lack. The film’s exploration of race, class, and sexuality deepens this analysis, revealing how desire is shaped by the larger social and cultural structures that define the boundaries between people.

## Aftersun

*Aftersun* (2022), directed by Charlotte Wells, is a deeply emotional, introspective film about memory, identity, and the bond between a father and daughter. The story unfolds primarily through the eyes of Sophie, an 11-year-old girl, as she reflects on a vacation she took with her father, Calum, when she was young. The film focuses on how Sophie, now an adult, revisits her memories to understand her father, who seems to be struggling with his inner world.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and the Unrepresentable** :
In Lacanian theory, the **Real**  refers to aspects of life and experience that cannot be fully captured or symbolized by language or representation. In *Aftersun*, much of Calum’s emotional life—his depression, sense of alienation, and internal struggles—remains unspoken and hidden from Sophie. As a child, Sophie perceives fragments of her father’s emotional state, but she cannot fully grasp or verbalize his inner turmoil. The film captures this gap between the child's perception and the adult’s hidden suffering, positioning Calum’s emotional state as part of the Real: something that resists full understanding and representation.
The absence of explicit explanation about Calum’s inner world mirrors the way the Real functions—it is a presence felt through the gaps, silences, and absences. Adult Sophie’s recollection of this vacation and her attempts to understand her father retroactively reflect a confrontation with the Real. She is trying to piece together the fragments of memory to make sense of something that remains elusive.
 
2. **The Imaginary and the Father-Daughter Relationship** :
The **Imaginary** , in Lacan’s theory, is the realm of images and ego formation, often tied to the mirror stage where the child forms an identity through identification with external images. Sophie’s relationship with her father is, in part, constructed through her childhood perception of him—he is a figure of admiration, protection, and mystery. However, as the film progresses, it becomes clear that Sophie’s image of her father, constructed through her memories, is incomplete.
Sophie’s idealized view of Calum in her childhood contrasts with the growing awareness of his vulnerabilities as she becomes an adult. The father-daughter bond is imbued with tenderness and care, but there are also moments where the fragility of the relationship is felt, particularly in Calum’s moments of solitude and emotional withdrawal. The film shows the limits of the Imaginary, where Sophie’s image of her father is shattered or incomplete, and she must confront a more complex, layered reality.
 
3. **The Symbolic and the Absence of the Father** :
The **Symbolic Order**  is the realm of language, societal norms, and law. In *Aftersun*, Calum’s role as a father is tied to his position within the Symbolic—he is expected to care for and provide for Sophie. Yet, Calum is shown to be deeply disconnected from these societal expectations. His financial struggles and emotional distance suggest that he feels inadequate within the Symbolic Order, unable to fully fulfill the role that is expected of him as a father.
The adult Sophie’s retrospection on her father suggests her own engagement with the Symbolic as she tries to understand him through the language and frameworks available to her as an adult. However, the film emphasizes the limits of this process. Despite Sophie’s efforts, there remains a fundamental gap between the father she remembers and the father she never fully knew. The film subtly critiques the societal pressures placed on Calum and how these pressures might have contributed to his emotional struggles, positioning him as a figure caught between the demands of the Symbolic and the experience of the Real.
 
4. **Desire and the Lack** :
Lacan’s concept of **lack**  is central to the formation of desire. In *Aftersun*, the film is marked by a profound sense of longing—both Sophie’s longing to understand her father and Calum’s unspoken desires and unmet needs. Calum’s quiet sadness, his moments of isolation, and his struggles with self-worth hint at a deeper sense of lack in his life. He is trying to provide Sophie with a joyful vacation, but there is an underlying sense of dissatisfaction or incompleteness in his life, which he cannot articulate or address.
For Sophie, both as a child and as an adult, there is a sense of longing to bridge the gap between her and her father, to understand his emotional world. This desire, however, is never fully satisfied, as the film suggests that some aspects of Calum’s experience remain unknowable. This reflects Lacan’s notion that desire is always structured around a lack—something is always missing, and it is this absence that drives the characters' actions and reflections.

#### Conclusion: 
*Aftersun* is a film that resonates with Lacanian concepts of the Real, the Imaginary, and the Symbolic. The father-daughter relationship is marked by gaps and silences, where much of the emotional depth remains unspoken but deeply felt. Sophie’s adult reflections on her childhood and her father represent a journey into the limits of memory, where she confronts the unattainability of fully understanding her father. The film delicately explores the complexity of desire, identity, and the unbridgeable gaps between people, offering a poignant exploration of how we relate to others and to ourselves.

## A Quiet Passion ✔️

*A Quiet Passion* (2016), directed by Terence Davies, is a biographical drama about the life of American poet Emily Dickinson, portrayed by Cynthia Nixon. The film explores Dickinson's inner world, her reclusive lifestyle, her religious skepticism, and her struggles with mental and physical health, all framed through her relationships with her family and society. The narrative deeply engages with the emotional and intellectual battles she faces, including her pursuit of artistic freedom within the constraints of 19th-century gender roles.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Unattainable** :
Lacan’s theory of desire revolves around the **objet petit a** , the unattainable object that continually drives human longing. In *A Quiet Passion*, Emily Dickinson’s life is marked by her insatiable desire for recognition and validation of her poetic work, which remains largely unfulfilled in her lifetime. Her poetry expresses a yearning for transcendence and meaning, yet she is constantly confronted by the limitations of human understanding, social conventions, and her own reclusiveness. The unattainable object in Dickinson’s case could be seen as the full recognition of her artistic genius, which she can never fully realize within the confines of her life.
 
2. **The Symbolic and the Restriction of Gender** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Symbolic Order**  represents the realm of language, societal norms, and law. Dickinson, as portrayed in the film, struggles within the rigid social structures of her time, particularly those related to gender. The expectations placed upon women in 19th-century America are suffocating for someone like Dickinson, who desires intellectual and creative freedom. Her rejection of marriage and her decision to live a reclusive life symbolize her resistance to the Symbolic structures that define femininity and domesticity in her society.
Despite this resistance, Dickinson is also deeply affected by the limitations imposed upon her as a woman and a poet. The Symbolic Order denies her the full expression of her desires, particularly in terms of literary recognition and intellectual equality with men. Her poetry, often introspective and focused on death, nature, and existence, becomes her form of rebellion against these limitations, yet it is within a structure that never fully acknowledges her as its equal.
 
3. **The Imaginary and Self-Perception** :
Lacan’s **Imaginary**  is the realm of images and identification, particularly in relation to how we see ourselves and others. Throughout the film, Dickinson grapples with her self-image and her place in the world. She is acutely aware of her difference from others—her sharp wit, emotional intensity, and desire for solitude mark her as an outsider in her family and social circle.
Her reclusiveness can be interpreted as a retreat into the Imaginary, where she constructs an internal world that allows her to maintain her sense of self in the face of societal pressures. The more she withdraws from the outside world, the more her identity is defined by this internal self-perception, which becomes both a source of strength and isolation. Her poetry, in a sense, functions as a mirror through which she reflects and shapes her identity, but it is also a space where she confronts the limits of language and meaning.
 
4. **The Real and Death** :
The **Real**  in Lacanian psychoanalysis refers to the aspects of life that cannot be fully symbolized or represented, including death. Dickinson’s poetry is obsessed with the theme of death, and *A Quiet Passion* delves into her existential struggles with mortality. Her reflections on death are not just philosophical but also deeply personal, as she contemplates the meaning of her own life and the inevitability of its end. This preoccupation with death can be seen as Dickinson’s attempt to engage with the Real, the unspeakable and ungraspable aspects of existence that lie beyond the reach of language and social structures.
The film portrays Dickinson’s deteriorating health and increasing reclusiveness as a confrontation with the Real, as she becomes more aware of her own mortality. The fear of death, and the longing for some form of transcendence or immortality through her poetry, reflect the ultimate tension between the Symbolic (language, societal norms) and the Real (death, the void).

#### Conclusion: 
*A Quiet Passion* offers a powerful depiction of Emily Dickinson’s life and work through a Lacanian lens, highlighting the central tensions between desire, identity, and societal constraints. Dickinson’s struggle to assert her creative voice within the confines of a rigid Symbolic Order, her reclusive retreat into the Imaginary, and her engagement with the Real through her preoccupation with death all speak to the core themes of Lacanian psychoanalysis. The film portrays her poetic genius as both a gift and a burden, as she navigates the unattainable desires and existential questions that define her life.

## Departure ✔️

*Departure* (2015), directed by Andrew Steggall, is a coming-of-age drama set in the French countryside, focusing on the emotional journey of a teenage boy, Elliot, and his mother, Beatrice, as they navigate significant transitions in their lives. Elliot is coming to terms with his sexuality, while Beatrice is coping with the breakdown of her marriage. The film delicately examines themes of identity, desire, loss, and family dynamics, all framed within an introspective and poetic visual style.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Lack** :
A central concept in Lacanian psychoanalysis is **desire** , structured around the notion of **lack** —something missing in a person's life that drives their actions. In *Departure*, Elliot’s burgeoning sexual desire for a local boy, Clément, represents his search for identity and emotional connection. Elliot’s desire is marked by uncertainty, as he struggles with his feelings in a state of adolescence, where identity formation is fluid and incomplete. His fascination with Clément, who represents an unattainable ideal, reflects Lacan’s idea that desire is always structured around what is absent or unreachable.
Beatrice, on the other hand, is dealing with her own unfulfilled desires. Her marriage is disintegrating, and she feels lost in her role as a wife and mother. Both characters, mother and son, are caught in the tension between what they want and what they lack, as they seek ways to reconcile their desires with their realities.
 
2. **The Imaginary and Mirror Stage** :
Lacan’s **Imaginary**  relates to the formation of the ego through identification with images, often starting with the **mirror stage** , where a child first recognizes themselves as a separate entity. In *Departure*, Elliot is at a stage in life where he is forming his identity by mirroring the world around him, particularly through his interactions with Clément. He projects his desires and insecurities onto Clément, constructing an idealized version of who he wants to be. The relationship is more about Elliot’s own internal development than a genuine connection with Clément, symbolizing the way the Imaginary shapes self-perception.
 
3. **The Symbolic Order and Family Structure** :
The **Symbolic Order** , in Lacanian theory, involves language, social norms, and the structures that define relationships, including family dynamics. In *Departure*, the breakdown of Elliot’s parents' marriage symbolizes a disruption in the traditional family structure that typically grounds individuals within the Symbolic Order. Beatrice's emotional unraveling reflects her inability to fulfill the societal role of wife and mother, leaving her feeling unmoored.For Elliot, the absence of a stable father figure and the collapsing family structure intensify his internal conflict. As he navigates his sexual awakening, there is no clear paternal figure to provide symbolic guidance or the **Name-of-the-Father** , which, in Lacanian terms, represents the authority that helps individuals integrate into society's norms and rules. Elliot is left to navigate these transitions on his own, creating a sense of disconnection from the Symbolic Order.
 
4. **The Real and Death** :
Lacan’s **Real**  refers to that which cannot be fully symbolized or represented in language, often related to trauma, death, or the limits of human experience. In *Departure*, there is a sense of impending loss that hovers over the characters, particularly with Beatrice’s emotional state and the metaphorical "death" of her marriage. The film’s melancholic tone and quiet, meditative pacing emphasize the characters’ confrontation with the Real—the ungraspable aspects of their lives that they cannot fully understand or control.
Elliot’s sexual awakening and Beatrice’s emotional unraveling both hint at deeper existential concerns, where the characters are forced to confront parts of themselves and their desires that defy easy categorization or explanation. The film’s use of water, mirrors, and reflective surfaces further evoke the idea that beneath the surface of their interactions lies an unspoken, unconscious world that both mother and son struggle to articulate.

#### Conclusion: 
*Departure* can be richly analyzed through a Lacanian psychoanalytic lens, particularly in terms of desire, identity formation, and family dynamics. Elliot’s coming-of-age journey reflects Lacan’s ideas about the formation of the self through the Imaginary and the unattainability of desire, while Beatrice’s struggles echo the challenges of maintaining one's identity within the constraints of the Symbolic Order. The film's introspective tone and exploration of loss, longing, and unspoken emotional depth resonate with Lacanian themes, making it a poignant meditation on human desire and the limits of understanding.

## Longtime Companion

*Longtime Companion* (1990), directed by Norman René, is a landmark film that chronicles the early years of the AIDS epidemic through the experiences of a group of friends in New York City. As one of the first mainstream films to directly address the impact of AIDS on the gay community, it presents a deeply emotional narrative that explores themes of love, loss, and the social changes brought on by the epidemic. The film follows several characters, including lovers, friends, and acquaintances, as they navigate the fear and devastation caused by the disease, eventually dealing with the grief and trauma of losing loved ones.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and Death** :
In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the **Real**  represents that which is outside language and impossible to fully comprehend or symbolize, particularly trauma, death, and existential realities. *Longtime Companion* portrays the characters' confrontation with death on an unprecedented scale as they lose their friends, partners, and themselves to AIDS. The omnipresence of death creates a confrontation with the Real, something that cannot be easily processed or assimilated within language or social structures. This confrontation is particularly evident in the characters' silent suffering and their attempts to find meaning in the face of the inexplicable and uncontrollable epidemic.
The film’s depiction of hospitals, funerals, and moments of quiet reflection emphasizes the pervasive sense of loss that eludes understanding. The grieving process, often shown through moments of silence, tears, or emotional outbursts, reflects the difficulty of articulating the trauma of witnessing so many deaths. The Real, in the form of the epidemic, tears through the Symbolic structures (language, social norms), leaving the characters to navigate this unspeakable grief.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Social Identity** :
Lacan's **Symbolic Order**  involves the social world of language, laws, and norms that structure human experience. In *Longtime Companion*, the gay characters find themselves on the margins of the Symbolic Order, particularly as they are stigmatized and marginalized by society due to their sexuality and the association of AIDS with the gay community. The fear and alienation they experience mirror the way societal norms fail to accommodate their experiences, forcing them into a position of exclusion.
The film also depicts how the characters attempt to create their own communities, support systems, and symbolic structures to cope with the crisis. They organize marches, form caregiving networks, and bond over shared losses. These actions represent their attempts to assert their place within the Symbolic Order despite the external pressures that seek to deny their identities and experiences. The term "longtime companion" itself, used as a euphemism for same-sex partners in obituaries during the early years of the epidemic, reflects the limitations of the Symbolic Order in recognizing their relationships and love.
 
3. **Desire and the Imaginary** :
In Lacan’s theory, **desire**  is always structured around a lack or absence, and it is through the **Imaginary**  (the realm of images and identifications) that individuals shape their desires and identities. The relationships depicted in *Longtime Companion* are filled with complex desires—both for romantic connection and for survival in the face of overwhelming loss. The longing for normalcy, love, and acceptance is ever-present as the characters watch their community dissolve around them.
Many of the characters attempt to find solace and meaning in their relationships, friendships, and personal identities, which become reflections of how they imagine themselves in the world. However, these desires are constantly thwarted by the presence of illness and death, revealing the gap between the idealized images they hold and the harsh reality of the epidemic.
 
4. **The Absence of the Father (Nom-du-Père)** :
The **Name-of-the-Father**  (Nom-du-Père) in Lacan’s theory refers to the paternal function that introduces individuals into the Symbolic Order, often by enforcing societal laws and norms. In *Longtime Companion*, the absence of societal recognition and support for the gay community during the AIDS crisis can be seen as a metaphorical lack of the paternal function. The government and medical institutions, which could represent the paternal authority, are slow to respond, leaving the characters to fend for themselves.
This absence of institutional support creates a void, forcing the characters to navigate their desires and grief without the external validation or guidance that might come from the wider social order. This lack intensifies their feelings of isolation and despair, but also pushes them toward creating their own sense of solidarity and community.

#### Conclusion: 
*Longtime Companion* can be analyzed through a Lacanian lens as a powerful exploration of desire, identity, and loss in the face of an overwhelming crisis. The film portrays the confrontation with the Real—death and trauma—as the characters grapple with the impact of the AIDS epidemic on their lives. At the same time, it explores the limitations of the Symbolic Order, particularly in recognizing and supporting marginalized communities. Through the lens of Lacan’s theories of desire and identity, the film becomes a poignant meditation on the human need for connection and meaning, even in the face of insurmountable loss.

## Tár

*Tár* (2022), directed by Todd Field, is a psychological drama that follows Lydia Tár (played by Cate Blanchett), a renowned and fictional classical music conductor and composer, as she navigates her professional success and personal unraveling. The film delves deeply into themes of power, artistic genius, and the moral consequences of one's actions, portraying Lydia's fall from grace as allegations of abuse and manipulation emerge.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and Trauma** :
In Lacanian theory, the **Real**  represents the unspeakable, unrepresentable trauma that cannot be fully grasped within the limits of language and societal structures. In *Tár*, Lydia's breakdown throughout the film can be interpreted as a confrontation with the Real. Her carefully constructed world of prestige, power, and artistic authority begins to collapse as past actions come back to haunt her. The unresolved tension between her personal desires and professional identity is exposed, leading her to confront the raw, uncontainable aspects of her psyche—fears, guilt, and the consequences of unchecked ambition.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Power** :
The **Symbolic Order** , according to Lacan, refers to the social structures, norms, and laws that shape human interaction. Lydia Tár is a figure who thrives within the Symbolic Order, particularly the male-dominated world of classical music. Her identity is shaped by the power dynamics of her position, as she navigates her role with an authority that both inspires admiration and fear.
However, as the film progresses, Lydia's relationship to the Symbolic Order becomes more precarious. The allegations against her reveal a darker side to her use of power, suggesting that she has manipulated her protégées and crossed ethical lines. Her downfall can be seen as a rupture in the Symbolic Order, where the societal norms that once upheld her status as a revered conductor now condemn her.
 
3. **Desire and the Other** :
Lacan’s theory of **desire**  posits that our desires are shaped by the **Other** —society, culture, and those around us. Lydia's desire for control, admiration, and recognition is evident throughout the film. She is driven by her need to assert her genius and dominance in a world where she must constantly prove herself, both as an artist and a woman in a traditionally male sphere. Her relationship with power and prestige is reflective of her unconscious desire to maintain her status in the eyes of the Other.
Lydia's desire is also entangled with her complex relationships, particularly with her protégées, such as Francesca (Noémie Merlant) and Krista, whose careers she has influenced. Her inability to maintain ethical boundaries in these relationships points to a deeper psychological need to control the narratives of those she mentors, making them extensions of her own desire for perfection and legacy.
 
4. **The Imaginary and Identity** :
The **Imaginary** , in Lacan’s framework, refers to the realm of images and the ego’s formation through identification with others. Lydia’s public persona as a brilliant conductor and cultural icon is an image she has meticulously crafted. Her identity is closely tied to this image, and much of the film shows how she performs this role with confidence and grandeur. However, as the story unfolds, the cracks in her self-image begin to appear.
Her fall from grace is not only a public spectacle but also a personal unraveling of her Imaginary self. The more Lydia loses control of her narrative, the more her sense of self deteriorates. The film’s use of haunting, surreal imagery, such as her recurring nightmares and hallucinations, emphasizes this disintegration of the Imaginary. Lydia is no longer able to sustain the idealized image of herself as a masterful artist; instead, she is confronted with the fragility of her identity.
 
5. **The Role of the Father (Nom-du-Père)** :
Lacan’s **Name-of-the-Father**  (Nom-du-Père) represents the authority that introduces individuals into the Symbolic Order and enforces societal rules. In *Tár*, Lydia herself occupies the paternal role within her professional domain. As a conductor, she exerts authority over her orchestra, shaping their performance and dictating their artistic vision. Her role is one of symbolic power, embodying the paternal function of order and control.
However, Lydia’s inability to balance this paternal authority with ethical responsibility leads to her undoing. Her abuse of power and her failure to uphold the moral standards expected of someone in her position represent a collapse of the paternal function. As a result, she is cast out from the very structures that once supported her authority.

#### Conclusion: 
*Tár* presents a rich narrative for Lacanian psychoanalytic analysis, particularly in terms of desire, power, and identity. Lydia Tár’s journey from the height of artistic success to personal and professional ruin reflects key Lacanian themes, such as the confrontation with the Real, the fragility of the Imaginary, and the collapse of the Symbolic Order. Through Lydia’s unraveling, the film explores the moral and psychological complexities of wielding power in the arts, making it a profound meditation on ambition, ethics, and the human condition.

## The Florida Project

*The Florida Project* (2017), directed by Sean Baker, is a poignant film that explores the lives of those on the margins of society, particularly focusing on a young girl, Moonee, and her mother, Halley, living in a budget motel near Disney World. Through the eyes of Moonee and her carefree summer, the film portrays the stark contrast between childhood innocence and the harsh economic realities of her family's situation.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and Economic Struggle** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Real**  represents that which cannot be fully expressed through language or understood through the Symbolic Order (social norms and systems). In *The Florida Project*, the Real manifests through the brutal economic conditions faced by Halley and the other motel residents. While Moonee remains largely unaware of these hardships, Halley’s life is filled with the trauma and anxiety of trying to survive on the edge of homelessness. The constant threat of eviction and Halley's illegal activities are symptomatic of a confrontation with the Real, as her precarious existence is something she cannot fully control or escape.
The motel’s proximity to Disney World—a symbol of fantasy, wealth, and escapism—heightens the tension between the Real and the imaginary ideal of the American Dream. The film suggests that for people like Halley, who exist on the fringes of society, this ideal is unattainable, and their economic struggles are the harsh, inescapable Real that looms over them.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Social Marginalization** :
Lacan’s **Symbolic Order**  represents the social structures, norms, and laws that regulate human life. In *The Florida Project*, Halley and the other motel residents are largely excluded from this order. They exist outside the traditional societal systems of stability—jobs, housing, and education—living in a transient space between survival and collapse. Halley's antagonistic relationship with authority figures, such as Bobby (the motel manager) and social services, illustrates her resistance to the societal structures that seek to control or contain her.
The motel itself acts as a liminal space, a site on the border of social inclusion and exclusion. The residents of the motel are, in many ways, invisible to the broader society, existing in the shadows of affluence. Their precarious existence challenges the myths of prosperity and success that dominate the Symbolic Order.
 
3. **The Imaginary and Childhood Innocence** :
In Lacan’s theory, the **Imaginary**  is the realm of images and identification, particularly tied to how individuals form their sense of self. Moonee’s experiences in the film are largely shaped by the Imaginary, as she navigates her world with wonder and adventure, despite the dire circumstances around her. For her, the dilapidated surroundings of the motel are transformed into playgrounds, and she is able to create an imaginative, carefree existence, unaware of the harsh realities that dominate her mother’s life.
Moonee's constant play and exploration represent a fantasy world that contrasts with the grim realities of adulthood, particularly her mother’s struggles. The film often juxtaposes Moonee’s imaginative world with the darker realities of her mother’s life, showing how children, through the lens of the Imaginary, can escape into a world of fantasy, even when their environment is bleak.
 
4. **The Father and the Absence of Authority** :
Lacan’s **Name-of-the-Father**  (Nom-du-Père) represents the authority that introduces individuals into the Symbolic Order and upholds societal laws and norms. In *The Florida Project*, the absence of paternal figures is significant, both literally and symbolically. Moonee's father is never present, and Halley is left to raise her daughter in an environment that lacks the traditional structures of family and authority.
Bobby, the motel manager, serves as a surrogate paternal figure. Although he is not Moonee's or Halley's father, he provides a form of symbolic authority, trying to maintain order within the motel while offering compassion to the residents. However, his authority is limited, and he is often caught between enforcing rules and sympathizing with the struggles of the residents. His role highlights the instability of the paternal function in this context, as the residents are constantly at risk of falling through the cracks of the social order.

#### Conclusion: 
*The Florida Project* offers a rich ground for Lacanian psychoanalysis, particularly in terms of how it explores economic precarity, social exclusion, and the contrasting realms of childhood fantasy and adult reality. Moonee’s imaginative life represents the escape provided by the Imaginary, while Halley’s struggles reflect a harsh confrontation with the Real, as she grapples with the economic and social forces that shape her existence. The absence of traditional paternal authority and the marginalization of the motel’s residents within the Symbolic Order underscore the film’s critique of societal structures that fail to support those living on the fringes. Through its nuanced portrayal of life at the edges of the American Dream, *The Florida Project* becomes a powerful exploration of desire, survival, and the search for meaning in the face of overwhelming adversity.

## The Worst Person in the World

*The Worst Person in the World* (2021), directed by Joachim Trier, is a Norwegian film that follows Julie (Renate Reinsve) as she navigates her twenties and thirties, grappling with love, identity, and the pressures of adulthood. The film is a character-driven exploration of existential angst and personal growth, divided into twelve chapters, an epilogue, and a prologue. It touches on themes of modern relationships, ambition, and the search for meaning in a chaotic world.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and Emotional Conflict** :
In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the **Real**  refers to the inescapable truths or traumas that resist being symbolized within language or societal structures. Julie’s journey in the film reflects her confrontation with the Real, particularly as she experiences the disillusionment of failed relationships, the realization of mortality, and the inability to find fulfillment. Her inability to commit to a single identity or life path suggests that she is grappling with the chaotic nature of life, which escapes easy categorization or understanding.
A crucial encounter with the Real occurs in the latter part of the film, when Julie’s ex-partner, Aksel, faces terminal illness. This moment exposes the raw and incomprehensible aspect of death and loss, pushing Julie into an emotional crisis that she cannot fully articulate. It represents the intrusion of the Real into her life, where the fantasy of endless choices and freedom is shattered by the inevitability of mortality.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Identity** :
Lacan’s **Symbolic Order**  involves the structures of language, societal expectations, and norms that shape human identity and relationships.
