Throughout *The Worst Person in the World*, Julie struggles to find her place within this order. She shifts between careers and relationships, reflecting her discomfort with the societal pressures of committing to a stable identity. The expectations of adulthood—career success, family, and relationships—loom over her, yet she continuously resists these conventions, feeling trapped by the roles others expect her to fill.
Her relationship with Aksel, a comic book artist in his 40s, represents her grappling with this tension. Aksel’s desire for stability and family contrasts with Julie’s restlessness and uncertainty. Their relationship becomes a battleground between the pull of societal norms (Aksel’s desire for children, commitment) and Julie’s resistance to being constrained by these expectations. Her ambivalence toward motherhood, for example, reflects her deeper anxiety about being subsumed into the Symbolic Order’s expectations of what a “woman” should be.
 
3. **The Imaginary and Desire** :
The **Imaginary** , according to Lacan, is the realm of images and idealized versions of the self, often shaped by identification with others. In the film, Julie’s desire is deeply connected to the Imaginary, as she struggles with who she is versus who she imagines she should be. Throughout her relationships, she projects different versions of herself—whether it’s in her passionate but chaotic connection with Eivind (Herbert Nordrum) or her more grounded relationship with Aksel. Julie is caught between the images she creates of herself and the reality of her actions and feelings.
The film’s use of surreal, fantastical elements, such as a scene where time literally stops for Julie to run through the streets of Oslo and pursue Eivind, highlights this tension between fantasy and reality. These moments reflect Julie’s internal desire for freedom and escape from the expectations placed upon her, but they also underscore the impossibility of living in the Imaginary without consequences in the Real.
 
4. **Desire and the Other** :
Lacan posits that desire is structured by the **Other** —the societal and cultural norms that shape what we want. Julie’s existential angst is fueled by her awareness of how her desires are shaped by the people around her, whether it’s the expectations of her parents, the needs of her partners, or the societal pressures of what she should achieve by a certain age. The title of the film, *The Worst Person in the World*, reflects her self-perception as someone who continually fails to live up to these external demands.
Julie’s relationships with Aksel and Eivind highlight her conflict with desire. Aksel’s traditional desires clash with Julie’s reluctance to settle down, while Eivind represents a more free-spirited, less conventional relationship. However, even in this dynamic, Julie struggles to define what she truly wants, as her desires seem to fluctuate based on who she is with. The Other’s influence over her choices becomes a source of anxiety and paralysis, as she remains uncertain about whether her desires are truly her own or merely a reflection of societal expectations.

#### Conclusion: 
*The Worst Person in the World* is a profound exploration of modern identity and desire through the lens of Lacanian psychoanalysis. Julie’s journey is marked by her confrontation with the Real, her resistance to the Symbolic Order, and her struggle with the fluctuating desires shaped by the Imaginary and the Other. The film captures the universal human experience of navigating the complexities of love, ambition, and self-identity in a world filled with conflicting demands. Through this lens, the film becomes a poignant reflection on the difficulty of defining oneself in the face of existential uncertainty.

## First Reformed ✔️

*First Reformed* (2017), directed by Paul Schrader, is a deeply introspective film that deals with themes of despair, faith, environmental crisis, and redemption. The story centers on Reverend Ernst Toller (Ethan Hawke), a minister at a small, historic church who grapples with personal and existential crises as he counsels a troubled parishioner, Michael, a radical environmentalist. As Toller becomes consumed by his own doubts and guilt, the film explores his psychological and spiritual deterioration.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and Ecological Crisis** :
Lacan’s **Real**  represents the ineffable and traumatic aspects of reality that cannot be fully integrated into the Symbolic Order (the societal structures of meaning). In *First Reformed*, the environmental crisis serves as the Real, something overwhelming and incomprehensible for Reverend Toller. The looming ecological disaster becomes a source of intense anxiety for both Michael, who ultimately commits suicide, and Toller, who begins to share Michael’s apocalyptic despair. This ecological trauma transcends rational discourse and becomes something that Toller cannot fully process through his faith or reason. His fixation on environmental destruction symbolizes his confrontation with the Real—something outside of language or institutional structures that he can’t fully control.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Faith** :
Lacan’s **Symbolic Order**  governs the systems of meaning, including law, religion, and language, through which individuals interpret the world. Toller, as a minister, is positioned within this symbolic structure of Christianity, which offers a framework for meaning, morality, and salvation. However, his growing disillusionment with both his faith and the larger religious institutions (like the corporate-backed megachurch Abundant Life) reveals a crisis in his relationship with the Symbolic Order.
Toller feels that his role as a pastor has become hollow, his sermons and religious rituals devoid of the power to address the pressing concerns of the modern world, particularly the impending environmental catastrophe. This gap between the religious Symbolic Order and the Real of ecological devastation leads to a crisis of faith. Toller begins to see the church as complicit in the destruction of the earth, especially through its connections with powerful polluters, further destabilizing his role within the Symbolic Order.
 
3. **The Imaginary and Identity** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Imaginary**  involves how individuals construct their identities through images and relationships, often tied to the ego. Toller’s identity is fractured from the beginning, shaped by the trauma of his past (the death of his son in the Iraq War) and his failed marriage. His sense of self as a minister is also in decline, as he is increasingly alienated from his congregation and from the institutional church. He feels irrelevant, isolated in his small, nearly empty church, much like the crumbling historic building itself.
As Toller takes on Michael’s concerns, his sense of self becomes increasingly aligned with radical environmentalism, and he starts to imagine himself as a martyr for a larger cause. His growing obsession with Michael’s ideals reflects an identity crisis, where Toller loses his grip on his previous self-image and becomes seduced by the idea of self-sacrifice for a higher, but destructive, purpose.
 
4. **Desire and the Other** :
Lacan’s concept of **desire**  is closely tied to the **Other** —the symbolic and social structures that shape what individuals want. Throughout *First Reformed*, Toller’s desires are largely shaped by his isolation and guilt. His desire for redemption is complicated by his feelings of inadequacy and despair, especially as he becomes obsessed with Michael’s apocalyptic worldview.
Toller’s relationship with Mary (Michael’s widow) offers a potential source of connection and redemption. However, his desire to protect and save her becomes intertwined with his own self-destructive impulses. He sees her as a figure of purity and salvation, but his inability to reconcile his desire for redemption with the overwhelming despair of the environmental crisis leads him to a violent and extreme plan.
 
5. **The Name-of-the-Father and Authority** :
Lacan’s **Name-of-the-Father**  (Nom-du-Père) represents the function of authority and the law that anchors individuals in the Symbolic Order. In *First Reformed*, Toller’s authority as a minister, a figure of moral and spiritual guidance, is undermined by his increasing radicalization and loss of faith in institutional religion. He is no longer able to uphold the paternal role of providing comfort and guidance to his congregation or even to himself.
The megachurch, Abundant Life, represents a corrupted version of the paternal authority, tied to corporate interests and disconnected from the spiritual crises that people like Toller and Michael face. The absence of genuine paternal authority leads Toller to take drastic actions, as he can no longer find redemption or meaning within the structures of the church or society.

#### Conclusion: 
*First Reformed* is a powerful exploration of despair, faith, and the search for meaning in the face of ecological disaster and personal trauma. Through the lens of Lacanian psychoanalysis, the film reveals the complex interplay between the Real (the ecological crisis), the Symbolic Order (religion and society), and the Imaginary (Toller’s fractured identity). Toller’s journey reflects the collapse of traditional structures of authority and meaning, leading to a confrontation with the Real that pushes him to the brink of self-destruction. The film ultimately poses profound questions about the role of faith, morality, and personal responsibility in an increasingly chaotic world.

## Portrait of a Lady on Fire ✔️

*Portrait of a Lady on Fire* (2019), directed by Céline Sciamma, is a French historical drama about the intense and forbidden romance between Marianne, a painter, and Héloïse, a reluctant bride-to-be, on a remote island in 18th-century France. The film explores themes of love, desire, gaze, and the dynamics of power and freedom within relationships. Through a Lacanian psychoanalytic lens, the film can be seen as a meditation on desire, identity, and the symbolic order.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Gaze and Desire** :
A central Lacanian concept in this film is the notion of the **gaze** . Lacan differentiates between the "look" and the "gaze," where the gaze is an awareness of being seen that disrupts the subject's sense of autonomy. In *Portrait of a Lady on Fire*, the film’s structure revolves around the act of looking—Marianne paints Héloïse, often without her knowledge, at first, which reflects the asymmetry of power. However, as their relationship develops, Héloïse’s awareness of being seen—and her active role in seeing Marianne—levels the power dynamics between them. The film ultimately deconstructs the traditional male gaze, offering a space where both characters are free to look at each other as equals, expressing desire outside of patriarchal structures.
The absence of men in much of the film allows the female gaze to dominate, creating a space where desire is mutual and not mediated by societal expectations or external judgment. This mutual gaze allows Marianne and Héloïse to discover their desires, shaping a relationship based on equality, complicity, and deep emotional connection.
 
2. **The Real and Forbidden Love** :
The **Real**  in Lacanian theory is that which resists symbolization, the unspeakable or impossible. The love between Marianne and Héloïse exists within the realm of the Real, as it is impossible within the Symbolic Order of the 18th century. Their love transcends the structures of society—marriage, gender roles, and the constraints placed on women—but it cannot survive within them. Their relationship is temporary and exists in a liminal space outside of the patriarchal order.
Their love is inherently tragic because it cannot be fully realized or symbolized within the world they inhabit. The final act of separation is inevitable, as Héloïse must return to the Symbolic Order through marriage, while Marianne must continue her life as an artist. Their love is therefore marked by an encounter with the Real—intense, transformative, but ultimately unsustainable within the confines of their society.
 
3. **The Symbolic Order and Marriage** :
The **Symbolic Order**  represents the societal structures, rules, and language that define identity and relationships. In *Portrait of a Lady on Fire*, marriage represents this oppressive symbolic structure that dictates Héloïse's future. She has no choice but to marry a man she has never met, as dictated by the social and familial expectations of her class and gender.
Héloïse’s resistance to being painted is symbolic of her resistance to being reduced to a mere object within the Symbolic Order—a wife, a passive figure in a painting made for the male gaze. Her eventual consent to be painted marks her acceptance of her fate, but also her desire to leave a trace of her true self behind with Marianne, beyond the constraints of her societal role.
 
4. **The Imaginary and Identity** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Imaginary**  involves how individuals form their sense of identity through images and relationships. Marianne and Héloïse’s relationship is forged through the Imaginary, as they construct each other’s identities through the act of painting and being painted. Marianne’s creation of Héloïse’s portrait is an attempt to capture her essence, while Héloïse’s growing awareness of Marianne’s gaze allows her to actively participate in shaping her own image.
Their relationship also allows both women to momentarily escape the constraints of their socially prescribed roles. Marianne, as an artist, breaks the traditional gender roles by working in a male-dominated field, while Héloïse resists the role of submissive bride. Together, they create an Imaginary space where they can define themselves outside of the Symbolic Order’s limitations.

#### Conclusion: 
*Portrait of a Lady on Fire* is a profound exploration of desire, identity, and the power dynamics of the gaze. Through a Lacanian lens, the film deconstructs the traditional male gaze and creates a space where two women can explore their desires freely, albeit temporarily, outside the structures of the Symbolic Order. Their love, existing in the Real, is transformative but ultimately impossible to sustain within the confines of their world. The film beautifully captures the tension between the freedom of desire and the constraints of societal expectations, leaving a lasting impression of the power of mutual recognition and the tragedy of love bound by time and circumstance.

## Women on the Verge of a Nervous Breakdown

*Women on the Verge of a Nervous Breakdown* (1988), directed by Pedro Almodóvar, is a dark comedy that explores themes of love, betrayal, and emotional chaos through the experiences of a woman, Pepa, whose life unravels after her lover leaves her. The film, set in Madrid, portrays a series of increasingly bizarre and humorous events that lead to a cathartic resolution.
#### Psychoanalytic Analysis (Lacanian) 
 
1. **The Role of Desire** :
Central to Lacan’s theory is the idea that desire is never truly satisfied—it is always driven by a lack. In *Women on the Verge*, Pepa's frantic search for her lover, Iván, reflects a classic Lacanian structure where desire fuels her actions. Iván, as the object of desire, represents not only a romantic relationship but also Pepa's need for validation and understanding. However, the film demonstrates how this desire leads to chaos and irrational behavior, much like how the Real disrupts the Symbolic in Lacan's theory.
 
2. **The Imaginary and the Gaze** :
In Lacanian psychoanalysis, the **Imaginary**  concerns identity formation through images and the gaze. Pepa’s emotional breakdown can be seen as a result of how she constructs her identity through Iván’s gaze. As she struggles to regain control, it becomes clear that her sense of self has been overly dependent on her lover’s approval and attention, which mirrors the destabilization of identity in Lacan’s concept of the mirror stage.
 
3. **The Symbolic and Communication** :
The **Symbolic Order**  in Lacanian theory is the domain of language, law, and societal norms. The film plays with communication and miscommunication as recurring themes. Pepa’s inability to reach Iván through phone calls and her eventual unraveling symbolizes the breakdown of the Symbolic Order in her life. Her crisis can be interpreted as a failure to find a stable point in the Symbolic, as she is unable to locate herself without the linguistic and emotional ties to her lover.
 
4. **The Absence of the Father (Nom-du-Père)** :
The absence of a true paternal figure or authority in *Women on the Verge* creates a space of chaos and uncertainty, reflecting Lacan’s concept of the **Name-of-the-Father** . Iván's abandonment of Pepa leaves her in a space of disorder where conventional social structures seem to break down. Without the stabilizing presence of the paternal function, Pepa’s world spirals into absurdity.

#### Conclusion: 
*Women on the Verge of a Nervous Breakdown* provides fertile ground for a Lacanian analysis of desire, identity, and symbolic breakdown. Through Pepa’s journey, the film humorously but poignantly captures the confusion and instability that arise when one's desire is frustrated and when communication breaks down in both romantic and social contexts.

## The Color Purple ✔️

*The Color Purple* (1985), directed by Steven Spielberg and based on Alice Walker's Pulitzer Prize-winning novel, tells the harrowing yet empowering story of Celie, an African American woman in the early 20th century South. It explores themes of racial and gender oppression, sexual abuse, self-discovery, and ultimately, empowerment. Through a Lacanian psychoanalytic lens, this film can be analyzed in terms of Celie’s journey from being silenced and oppressed to finding her voice and subjectivity.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Symbolic Order and Oppression** :
Celie’s initial role in the film can be seen as embedded within the **Symbolic Order** , which represents societal structures, laws, and language that define and control individuals. At the start of the story, Celie is subjected to the oppressive authority of men, particularly her abusive stepfather and later her husband, Mister. She has no voice or agency and is silenced by the patriarchy. Lacan's concept of the **Name-of-the-Father** —the symbolic function that introduces the subject to the social order—can be applied to the oppressive male figures who define her place in society. They restrict her ability to develop her own identity outside the roles of wife and servant.
 
2. **The Imaginary and Identity Formation** :
Lacan’s **Imaginary**  is the realm of identity construction through images and relationships, particularly the mirror stage, where a person identifies with an image of themselves. For much of the film, Celie’s identity is shaped by how others perceive her, especially through the gaze of men who see her as an object. Her self-worth is deeply tied to the abusive relationships she endures, and she remains trapped in a cycle of self-hatred and submission.
However, her connection with other women—like Shug Avery and Sofia—provides her with alternative mirrors in which she can begin to see herself differently. Shug, in particular, plays a transformative role in helping Celie recognize her own beauty, sexuality, and strength, allowing her to redefine her identity outside of patriarchal and racist structures.
 
3. **The Real and Trauma** :
The **Real**  in Lacanian terms refers to what resists symbolization—the raw, traumatic experiences that cannot be fully articulated or processed. Celie’s experiences of abuse, incest, and separation from her children exist in the realm of the Real; they are traumatic events that haunt her throughout her life. These traumas are initially repressed or unspeakable, but as Celie gains agency and begins to voice her story through letters and speech, she begins to confront the Real, integrating it into her new sense of self.
Her final letter-writing serves as a form of communication that bridges the gap between the Real and the Symbolic, as it allows her to articulate her trauma and reclaim her identity.
 
4. **Desire and Subjectivity** :
Lacan’s theory of **desire**  is central to the formation of subjectivity. For much of the film, Celie’s desires are either suppressed or subordinated to the desires of others, particularly men. She has little control over her body or her fate. However, as she begins to develop relationships with women—particularly Shug—she starts to assert her own desires. These desires are not only sexual but also for independence, self-worth, and connection.
The relationship between Celie and Shug becomes a key moment of self-discovery, allowing Celie to articulate her own desires for the first time. Shug’s love and affirmation help Celie move from a position of passive object to active subject, reclaiming her sense of agency and autonomy.

#### Conclusion: 
*The Color Purple* can be understood through Lacanian psychoanalysis as a journey of transformation from being trapped in the oppressive structures of the Symbolic Order, to discovering identity and subjectivity through the Imaginary and the confrontation with the Real. Celie’s evolution from a silenced, passive figure to an empowered woman represents her breaking free from patriarchal control and reclaiming her voice and desires.

## The End of the Affair ✔️

*The End of the Affair* (1999), directed by Neil Jordan and based on Graham Greene’s novel, is a romantic drama set in post-war London. The film centers on a love triangle between Sarah, her husband Henry, and Maurice, a writer with whom she has an affair. At its heart, the film explores themes of love, faith, jealousy, and the intersection of passion and spirituality. A Lacanian psychoanalytic analysis would delve into how desire, the symbolic order, and the Real play out in the film’s narrative.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Object of Desire** :
Lacan’s concept of **desire**  is central to the dynamics between Sarah and Maurice. Maurice is driven by an insatiable desire for Sarah, but his obsession is not solely with her as a person. Rather, Sarah becomes the **objet petit a**  (object of desire) in Lacanian terms—an unattainable symbol of what he lacks. Maurice’s longing for Sarah represents his need to fill a void within himself, a desire that intensifies as she becomes more distant and mysterious.Sarah’s affair with Maurice is also tied to desire, but her motivations evolve from a romantic and physical desire to something more spiritual. Her growing belief in God complicates her feelings, as she becomes torn between human passion and divine devotion. In Lacanian terms, Sarah shifts from pursuing desire in the realm of the **Imaginary**  (romantic love) to grappling with the **Symbolic**  (faith, morality).
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Guilt** :
The **Symbolic Order** , with its societal laws and moral structures, plays a crucial role in Sarah’s internal conflict. Her affair with Maurice violates the societal and marital codes represented by her marriage to Henry. Yet, it is not just the betrayal of her husband that troubles Sarah, but her growing sense of guilt and fear that God is watching her. Lacan’s concept of the **Name-of-the-Father**  (Nom-du-Père) represents the authority that introduces individuals to societal laws, and in Sarah’s case, this authority is embodied in her developing faith.Sarah’s relationship with God becomes the ultimate **Big Other** —a force that represents the symbolic authority watching and judging her actions. Her promise to God to end the affair after believing Maurice survived a bombing because of divine intervention forces her to confront her guilt and sacrifice her personal desires for a higher moral calling.
 
3. **The Real and Trauma** :
The **Real**  in Lacanian theory refers to that which cannot be fully symbolized or articulated. The traumatic rupture in Maurice and Sarah’s affair occurs when she abruptly ends their relationship after making a vow to God. For Maurice, this unexplained abandonment thrusts him into the **Real** —a space of loss and confusion where the order of his world is shattered. He cannot rationalize Sarah’s actions within the Symbolic Order, as her decision is motivated by an encounter with the Real (her experience with faith and the miraculous survival of Maurice).
Maurice’s obsession with discovering the truth behind Sarah’s departure reflects his inability to come to terms with the trauma of losing her. The unknowable nature of her promise to God represents the Real, a force that resists symbolization and leaves him in a state of emotional disarray.
 
4. **Faith and the Beyond** :
Lacan’s notion of **jouissance**  (excessive pleasure or suffering) can be applied to Sarah’s journey. Her decision to renounce her love for Maurice and devote herself to God is an example of a higher form of jouissance, where she chooses spiritual fulfillment over physical or emotional pleasure. This shift reflects Lacan’s concept that true satisfaction lies not in the fulfillment of desire, but in its renunciation for something greater.
Sarah’s faith brings her a form of peace, but it also leads to suffering, as she is torn between her love for Maurice and her commitment to God. In the end, her death represents the ultimate submission to the Other (God), leaving Maurice and Henry to grapple with the void left in her absence.

#### Conclusion: 
*The End of the Affair* offers a rich landscape for Lacanian psychoanalytic interpretation, particularly in its exploration of desire, guilt, and the tension between love and faith. The film’s portrayal of Sarah’s internal struggle and Maurice’s obsessive search for answers reveals how individuals navigate the Real, the Symbolic, and the Imaginary in their pursuit of meaning and fulfillment. Ultimately, the film suggests that the answers to human longing lie not in the satisfaction of desire, but in confronting the limits imposed by morality, faith, and the unknowable aspects of existence.

## Wittgenstein, movie

*Wittgenstein* (1993), directed by Derek Jarman, is a biographical film about the Austrian-British philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein. The movie takes an unconventional approach, combining minimalist sets with rich dialogue to explore Wittgenstein’s life and philosophical ideas, particularly his work on language, logic, and the limits of meaning.
#### Analysis Through Wittgenstein's Philosophy: 
 
1. **Language and Meaning** :
Wittgenstein's central philosophy in his later works, especially in *Philosophical Investigations*, revolves around the idea that meaning is not inherent in words but is determined by their use in language games. The film illustrates this concept through various scenes where Wittgenstein discusses the relationship between language and reality, focusing on how words function in everyday life, rather than in abstract, theoretical terms. The film portrays Wittgenstein's growing frustration with the limitations of language and its inability to capture the totality of human experience.
 
2. **Private Language and the Mind** :
A key concept in Wittgenstein’s philosophy is his critique of the idea of a "private language"—a language that could describe one's internal, private experiences, like pain, in a way only the speaker could understand. The film explores this through Wittgenstein's interactions with his students and contemporaries, emphasizing the communal nature of language and meaning. Wittgenstein argues that language is inherently public; without shared understanding, communication would break down, which is visually and narratively emphasized throughout the film.
 
3. **Philosophy as Therapy** :
In Wittgenstein's later philosophy, he viewed philosophy not as a doctrine or theory but as a form of therapy, helping to dissolve the confusion brought about by misunderstandings of language. The film highlights Wittgenstein’s struggle with his own intellectual and emotional turmoil. It shows his philosophical work as a personal quest to resolve the contradictions and limitations he finds in both language and life, presenting philosophy as a tool for clarity rather than a system of thought.
 
4. **Minimalism and Symbolism in the Film** :
Jarman’s minimalist approach—using few props and abstract settings—reflects Wittgenstein's own drive toward clarity and simplicity in philosophical thinking. The film often uses stark, theatrical setups to strip down the narrative, focusing the viewer's attention on dialogue and ideas. The use of color and light in the film also serves as a symbolic representation of Wittgenstein's mental states and his quest for understanding the world through logic and language.
 
5. **Inner Turmoil and Isolation** :
Wittgenstein is portrayed as a deeply introspective and isolated figure, torn between his intellectual pursuits and his emotional struggles. The film explores his relationships, particularly with his students and close friends, depicting the tension between his philosophical rigor and his personal vulnerability. His alienation is reflected in his philosophical work, which often touches on the limits of communication and understanding, both personally and linguistically.

#### Conclusion: 
*Wittgenstein* is an intellectual and artistic reflection of the philosopher’s ideas on language, meaning, and human experience. Through its non-traditional narrative and visual style, the film captures the essence of Wittgenstein's thinking and personal struggles, offering viewers a meditative insight into the mind of one of the 20th century’s most influential philosophers.

## Ryans Totcher ✔️

It seems you're referring to "Ryan's Daughter," which is a 1970 British epic romantic drama directed by David Lean. If this was a different request, please clarify.
*Ryan's Daughter* (1970) - Summary and Analysis:*Ryan's Daughter* is set in a small Irish village during World War I and revolves around Rosy Ryan, a young woman married to a schoolteacher, Charles. She becomes involved in an affair with a British officer, Major Randolph Doryan, which ultimately brings about personal and social turmoil. The film explores themes of forbidden love, betrayal, and the consequences of personal desires conflicting with social norms.
#### Psychoanalytic/Lacanian Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Real** :
Rosy's affair with Doryan symbolizes her attempt to escape the banality of village life and the constraints of her marriage. In Lacanian terms, her desire for the Major represents an attempt to grasp the **Real** —a realm of raw, unmediated experience beyond the Symbolic (social norms and marital obligations). Her desire is driven by a fantasy of passion and escape, but it ultimately results in suffering and social alienation.
 
2. **The Symbolic and Authority** :
Rosy's husband, Charles, represents the Symbolic Order, embodying the societal expectations of a stable, conservative marriage. Her affair challenges these structures, leading to moral and societal judgment from the village. The tensions between her personal desires and the community’s collective values highlight the conflict between individual longing and social repression.
 
3. **The Big Other and Guilt** :
The village itself acts as the **Big Other** —the societal gaze that judges and condemns Rosy's actions. In Lacanian terms, Rosy's guilt arises not just from the affair, but from the social consequences of breaking the rules dictated by the Big Other. The film's tragic trajectory can be viewed as Rosy's punishment for stepping outside the boundaries set by this societal authority.

#### Conclusion: 
*Ryan's Daughter* is a poignant exploration of individual desire clashing with societal expectations. Through Rosy's affair and its tragic fallout, the film examines the cost of pursuing forbidden love in a rigid and judgmental society, making it a rich subject for psychoanalytic analysis.

## Les quatre cents coups ✔️

*Les Quatre Cents Coups* (1959), directed by François Truffaut, is a cornerstone of the French New Wave movement and follows the story of a young boy, Antoine Doinel, who struggles with neglect at home and alienation at school. The film portrays his rebellion against authority, his delinquency, and his yearning for freedom.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and Lack** :
In Lacanian theory, desire is shaped by a fundamental **lack** . Antoine's actions throughout the film reflect this void, as he searches for love, recognition, and freedom. His strained relationship with his parents, especially his mother, symbolizes the absence of emotional nurturing, fueling his sense of rejection and abandonment. This lack manifests in his defiance of authority and his quest for autonomy, seeking satisfaction outside of traditional family and school structures.
 
2. **The Imaginary and Identity Formation** :
Lacan’s **Imaginary Order**  is where the ego is constructed through identification with images. Antoine is at a stage of self-discovery, trying to define his identity in a world that consistently imposes limitations on him. His desire to run away, to break free from constraints, is a reflection of his struggle to assert his individuality. The scenes where Antoine fantasizes about escape, or gazes longingly at the sea, suggest his attempt to reconcile his fragmented identity with the unattainable notion of freedom.
 
3. **The Symbolic Order and Rebellion** :
The **Symbolic Order**  in Lacanian theory is where societal rules, language, and authority come into play. Antoine’s relationship with the Symbolic is fraught with tension. He constantly rebels against the social institutions—school, family, and legal authorities—that attempt to discipline and control him. His delinquent acts, such as skipping school and stealing, are attempts to reject the rules imposed by these structures. His rebellion can be understood as an attempt to carve out his own space outside the confines of the Symbolic Order, but this inevitably leads to his marginalization and punishment.
 
4. **The Real and Escape** :
The **Real**  in Lacan's framework is what cannot be symbolized or fully represented; it’s the raw experience that disrupts the social order. Antoine’s final escape to the beach in the iconic closing shot can be read as his confrontation with the Real. He runs towards the sea, symbolizing a moment of freedom but also uncertainty, as the vastness of the ocean represents the unknown and the unattainable. This ending leaves Antoine's fate unresolved, reflecting his ongoing struggle with the limitations imposed by society and his internal quest for meaning.

#### Conclusion: 
Through Antoine’s journey in *Les Quatre Cents Coups*, Truffaut provides a poignant exploration of adolescence, rebellion, and the search for identity. A Lacanian reading highlights how Antoine's actions are driven by a deep sense of lack and desire for freedom, while also showing how he is constrained by societal norms and authority. His final confrontation with the Real suggests that true freedom might be beyond reach, but it remains a powerful force in shaping his identity.

## Kes ✔️

*Kes* (1969), directed by Ken Loach, is a British social realist film about Billy Casper, a working-class boy in Northern England who finds solace in training a kestrel (hawk) amidst a life of neglect and harshness. The film is a powerful portrayal of class struggle, the brutality of adolescence, and the possibility of freedom in a constrained world.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Symbolic Order and Class Struggle** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Symbolic Order**  represents the realm of societal rules, language, and authority. Billy’s environment, characterized by a working-class setting, oppressive teachers, and a harsh family life, imposes restrictive rules and expectations on him. The school system, represented by authoritarian figures like Mr. Sugden (the gym teacher), mirrors the broader societal structure that keeps Billy and others of his class trapped in a cycle of low expectations and limited opportunities. The kestrel, in contrast, becomes a symbol of escape from the Symbolic’s oppressive boundaries, offering Billy a brief encounter with a world beyond his constrained existence.
 
2. **The Imaginary and Identification** :
Lacan's **Imaginary Order**  revolves around the formation of the self through identification. Billy's relationship with the kestrel is emblematic of his desire for self-identification and freedom. In caring for the bird, Billy creates a bond that allows him to see himself reflected in the kestrel’s ability to soar freely, something he cannot do in his own life. The kestrel becomes part of Billy’s Imaginary, an idealized version of what he wishes he could be: independent, graceful, and unbounded by the constraints of his socioeconomic reality.
 
3. **The Real and Loss** :
Lacan’s **Real**  represents the inexpressible and traumatic aspects of life that cannot be fully captured within language or the Symbolic Order. The ending of *Kes* confronts Billy with the harsh intrusion of the Real when his kestrel is killed by his brother Jud. This act of destruction represents a shattering of Billy’s fragile hope and escape, forcing him to confront the brutal reality of his life. The death of Kes symbolizes the impossibility of sustaining a connection to something outside the harsh structures that govern his world, forcing him to face the limits of his agency and freedom.
 
4. **Desire and the Object of Desire** :
The kestrel in *Kes* represents an **objet petit a**  in Lacanian theory—the unattainable object of desire that promises fulfillment. For Billy, Kes embodies his desire for something beyond the bleakness of his existence, a way to assert control and mastery in a world where he otherwise feels powerless. However, like all objects of desire in Lacanian thought, Kes is ultimately lost, revealing the inherent lack that drives Billy’s yearning. The kestrel is both a symbol of hope and an acknowledgment of the impossibility of ever fully escaping the limitations imposed on him by society.

#### Conclusion: 
In *Kes*, Ken Loach creates a poignant exploration of working-class life, using the relationship between Billy and his kestrel as a metaphor for the tension between freedom and social constraint. A Lacanian reading underscores the film’s portrayal of desire, loss, and the limits of individual agency within a rigid societal structure, revealing the complexity of Billy’s psychological and emotional world amidst the harshness of his reality.

## Dolor y Gloria, Lacanian analysis

In *Dolor y Gloria* (2019), directed by Pedro Almodóvar, the story centers around Salvador Mallo, a film director reflecting on his life, art, and relationships. Almodóvar uses Mallo's physical and emotional pain to explore themes of memory, identity, and creativity.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Symbolic Order and Desire** :
Lacan's **Symbolic Order** , representing societal structures and language, plays a crucial role in Salvador’s life. As a filmmaker, Salvador navigates the expectations and constraints placed upon him by the film industry, his past, and his relationships. His pain (both physical and emotional) reflects his struggle to reconcile his personal desires with the demands of the Symbolic Order, particularly the creative expectations placed on him. The film suggests that Salvador's blocked creativity is tied to his unresolved personal traumas, especially those related to his family and past lovers, which are rooted in the Symbolic realm of cultural and familial expectations.
 
2. **Desire and the Objet Petit a** :
In Lacanian theory, **desire**  is driven by an unattainable object known as the **objet petit a** . Salvador's desire revolves around reclaiming a sense of wholeness and fulfillment that he feels he has lost over the years. This is manifested in his search for meaning through relationships, memories, and his attempts to re-engage with his past creative self. His reconnection with Federico (his former lover) and the re-release of one of his earlier films highlight Salvador’s nostalgic longing for a time when his desires seemed more attainable. However, like the **objet petit a** , this sense of fulfillment remains elusive, and Salvador’s journey is marked by a recognition of the gaps in his life.
 
3. **The Real and Trauma** :
The **Real**  in Lacanian thought refers to the raw, traumatic elements of life that cannot be fully articulated in language. Salvador’s chronic pain, heroin addiction, and his inability to work signify the intrusion of the Real into his life. These elements are unmanageable and defy the structure of the Symbolic. The film frequently deals with the inexpressible—his bodily suffering, his mother’s death, and his childhood poverty—which permeates his present and shapes his artistic and emotional stagnation.
 
4. **The Imaginary and Maternal Figure** :
In Lacanian theory, the **Imaginary Order**  involves the formation of identity through relationships and images, particularly in relation to the maternal figure. Salvador’s memories of his mother, Jacinta, play a central role in shaping his emotional world. His frequent flashbacks to his childhood, especially moments with his mother, point to the profound influence of the maternal figure on his psyche. The unresolved tension with his mother, particularly her disappointment in him, haunts Salvador, symbolizing his struggle to reconcile his present self with the idealized image of himself that his mother had envisioned.
 
5. **The Role of Art as a Mediator** :
Art, in Lacanian terms, functions as a form of mediation between the Real and the Symbolic. Salvador’s art allows him to process his traumas and navigate his desires. The film is meta-textual in that it mirrors Almodóvar’s own life and artistic struggles, blending reality and fiction. Salvador's eventual return to filmmaking at the end of the movie can be seen as a Lacanian act of sublimation, where art becomes a way to channel and process his desires, pain, and past traumas.

#### Conclusion: 
*Dolor y Gloria* is a rich text for Lacanian analysis, as it delves into the intersections of desire, memory, trauma, and artistic creation. Through Salvador’s journey, the film explores the ways in which unresolved desires and traumas continue to influence one's life and creativity, while also highlighting the therapeutic role that art can play in confronting the inexpressible Real.

## God's own country

*God's Own Country* (2017), directed by Francis Lee, is a poignant British drama that explores themes of identity, isolation, and love. The story centers around Johnny Saxby, a young farmer burdened by his family responsibilities, who engages in self-destructive behavior until Gheorghe, a Romanian migrant worker, comes into his life, offering him emotional and physical intimacy.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Real** :
Lacanian theory posits that **desire**  is born out of a fundamental **lack** , an unfulfilled longing. Johnny's reckless behavior, casual sexual encounters, and excessive drinking reflect his attempt to fill this internal void. His disconnection from his family and emotional life indicates that he is deeply alienated from his desires, unable to articulate or even recognize them fully. Gheorghe’s arrival represents an encounter with the **Real** —an intrusion of something raw, authentic, and outside the established order of Johnny’s life. Through Gheorghe, Johnny confronts desires he has repressed, which are painful and disruptive, but also necessary for his emotional growth.
 
2. **The Imaginary and the Search for Identity** :
In Lacan's **Imaginary Order** , identity is constructed through mirroring and identification. At the beginning of the film, Johnny’s identity is built on isolation and a rugged, masculine ideal of self-reliance, as defined by his environment. His emotional numbness and his inability to communicate effectively are reflections of a fragmented self. Gheorghe offers Johnny a mirror through which he can begin to see a different version of himself—one that is capable of emotional vulnerability and intimacy. The physicality of their relationship becomes a way for Johnny to rebuild his fragmented sense of self, shifting from a place of alienation to one of connection.
 
3. **The Symbolic Order and the Role of Family** :
The **Symbolic Order**  in Lacanian thought refers to the structures and rules of society, such as language, family, and social expectations. Johnny’s family farm represents a harsh, duty-bound existence, defined by his ailing father’s expectations and the burden of maintaining the land. The farm acts as the primary symbol of the restrictive forces in Johnny’s life, trapping him within a particular narrative of masculinity and obligation. Gheorghe’s presence disrupts this order, allowing Johnny to question the values imposed upon him by his family and environment. This disruption challenges the traditional Symbolic structure of what it means to be a man and a farmer, allowing Johnny to redefine his place within this order.
 
4. **The Other and Acceptance** :
Lacan’s concept of the **Other**  refers to external forces or individuals who shape our self-conception. Gheorghe, as a migrant worker, occupies the position of the **Other**  in both a cultural and personal sense. Initially, Johnny views Gheorghe through the lens of prejudice, seeing him as an outsider to be mistrusted. However, as their relationship develops, Gheorghe becomes a vital figure through whom Johnny learns to accept himself and confront his vulnerabilities. This transformation signifies Johnny's growing ability to move beyond the limitations imposed by his environment and engage with the Other in a more open and authentic way.
 
5. **Sublimation and Reconciliation** :
Lacan's concept of **sublimation**  refers to the process by which desires are redirected into more socially acceptable forms. In *God's Own Country*, Johnny’s journey can be seen as one of sublimation, where his initially destructive desires (expressed through alcohol and meaningless encounters) are transformed into a more productive form of love and intimacy. The land, initially a symbol of burden, also becomes a space of reconciliation, as Johnny begins to repair his relationship with both Gheorghe and his family by the end of the film, suggesting a newfound harmony between his desires and his duties.

#### Conclusion: 
*God's Own Country* is a deeply emotional exploration of desire, identity, and transformation. Through a Lacanian lens, the film captures the painful yet necessary confrontation with the Real, as Johnny's suppressed desires come to the surface through his relationship with Gheorghe. His journey from isolation to emotional intimacy highlights the tension between the symbolic structures that confine him and the liberating power of love, offering a complex and layered narrative of personal and relational growth.

## Of an age

*Of an Age* (2022), directed by Goran Stolevski, is an Australian queer romance film that follows the intense 24-hour connection between Kol, a Serbian-born ballroom dancer, and Adam, his friend’s older brother. The film deals with themes of identity, desire, nostalgia, and the tension between fleeting moments of intimacy and the weight of time.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **Desire and the Unattainable Object** :
In Lacan’s theory, **desire**  is always structured around a sense of lack, which is never fully satisfied. In *Of an Age*, Kol’s desire for Adam becomes a pivotal part of his emotional growth. Their connection is passionate but transient, emphasizing the **objet petit a** , or the unattainable object of desire, that Kol seeks but cannot possess fully. The briefness of their encounter, marked by deep emotional resonance, creates a sense of unfulfilled desire that haunts Kol beyond their time together. This fleeting connection leaves a gap in Kol’s life, symbolizing Lacan's idea that desire is always driven by what cannot be fully attained.
 
2. **The Imaginary and Identification** :
Lacan’s **Imaginary Order**  is where one’s sense of self is constructed through identification. Kol’s identity is in flux throughout the film, shaped by his relationship with Adam and his internal conflict over his cultural and sexual identity. Adam serves as a mirror for Kol, representing an idealized version of what Kol yearns to be—free, expressive, and emotionally available. This identification with Adam allows Kol to momentarily escape the constraints of his cultural background and embrace a more fluid sense of self, even if only for a short time.
 
3. **The Symbolic Order and Social Constraints** :
The **Symbolic Order**  refers to the societal structures and language that impose limits on individual desires. Kol’s immigrant background and his ties to Serbian culture impose certain expectations and norms, particularly regarding masculinity and sexuality. His relationship with Adam challenges these boundaries, representing a break from the restrictive social expectations placed upon him. However, this defiance is temporary, as Kol must eventually return to his everyday life, where these social structures reassert themselves. The fleeting nature of his relationship with Adam underscores the difficulty of fully escaping the Symbolic Order's constraints, especially in the context of queer love.
 
4. **Time and the Real** :
The brief, intense relationship between Kol and Adam confronts Kol with the **Real** , the traumatic and raw aspects of life that disrupt the Symbolic Order. Time plays a crucial role in this confrontation, as the two characters' connection exists within the limits of a single day. This temporality heightens the emotional stakes of their encounter, forcing Kol to confront the fragility of love and connection. The Real, in this sense, is the recognition of time’s unstoppable progression and the impossibility of holding onto something forever. This realization leaves Kol with a sense of loss and a deep, unresolved yearning.
 
5. **Sublimation through Memory** :
Lacan’s concept of **sublimation**  refers to the transformation of desires into something socially or personally meaningful. For Kol, his relationship with Adam becomes sublimated through memory. While the connection was short-lived, its emotional impact is profound, allowing Kol to transform this experience into a source of emotional and psychological growth. The film suggests that, although Kol cannot sustain the relationship, its memory will continue to shape his future, symbolizing the way in which moments of desire and intimacy can be preserved and reinterpreted over time.

#### Conclusion: 
Through a Lacanian lens, *Of an Age* is an exploration of fleeting desire, identity formation, and the tension between personal freedom and societal constraints. Kol’s journey with Adam serves as a reflection of his deeper internal struggles with his own identity and the unattainable nature of desire, while also revealing the emotional depths that brief connections can leave behind. The film ultimately reflects the painful beauty of moments that cannot be sustained, offering insight into how these experiences shape one's sense of self and future.

## Juste le fin DU monde

*Juste la fin du monde* (2016), directed by Xavier Dolan, is a film about a young writer, Louis, returning home after 12 years of estrangement to tell his family that he is dying. The story is adapted from Jean-Luc Lagarce's play of the same name and explores themes of communication, familial tension, and isolation.
#### Lacanian Psychoanalytic Analysis: 
 
1. **The Real and the Impossible Communication** :
In Lacanian theory, the **Real**  refers to what is beyond language and representation—the inexpressible or traumatic. In *Juste la fin du monde*, Louis’s inability to communicate his imminent death to his family represents the intrusion of the Real into the Symbolic Order of family and language. Despite his intention to share this profound truth, every attempt at conversation devolves into misunderstanding, argument, or avoidance, highlighting the impossibility of articulating something as traumatic as one's own mortality. This confrontation with the Real underscores the limits of language and familial bonds.
 
2. **The Symbolic Order and Family Dynamics** :
The **Symbolic Order** , governed by social structures and language, is embodied in the family’s dysfunctional communication. Each family member is trapped in their roles and histories, governed by unspoken rules that inhibit genuine connection. Louis’s siblings and mother represent the societal and familial structures that have defined his identity, but they fail to understand his silence and withdrawal. In this sense, the family is a microcosm of the Symbolic, where roles and expectations constrain authentic interaction. Louis’s return disrupts this fragile equilibrium, but his inability to break free from these roles ultimately results in a failed attempt at reconciliation.
 
3. **The Imaginary and Fragmented Selves** :
In Lacanian terms, the **Imaginary Order**  relates to the formation of identity through images and relationships. Louis’s estrangement from his family reflects a fragmented sense of self. His prolonged absence has allowed his family members to project their own fantasies and frustrations onto him, creating an idealized or demonized image of who he is. Throughout the film, the interactions reveal not Louis as he truly is, but rather the distorted versions of him as imagined by his family. His silence further fuels these projections, leaving him trapped in an imagined version of himself that is disconnected from his true desires and reality.
 
4. **Desire and the Unattainable** :
Lacan posits that **desire**  is structured around a fundamental lack, represented by the unattainable **objet petit a** . In *Juste la fin du monde*, Louis’s desire for understanding and acceptance from his family remains elusive. His return home is driven by a desire to be seen and understood, yet the family’s inability to truly hear him or grasp the gravity of his situation leaves him isolated. The gap between what Louis wants (authentic connection) and what he receives (miscommunication and conflict) creates a deep sense of frustration and loss. This unattainable desire is central to the film’s emotional tension, as Louis grapples with the impossibility of being fully understood.
 
5. **The Gaze and the Other** :
Lacan’s concept of the **gaze**  represents the way in which we are seen and defined by the **Other** . Throughout the film, Louis is subjected to the gaze of his family, who project their unresolved issues onto him. His return home does not elicit sympathy or connection but rather exacerbates existing tensions. This gaze strips Louis of agency, as he becomes an object within his family’s emotional struggles rather than an individual with his own needs. His family’s inability to see him for who he truly is represents the failure of the Other to recognize the subject's true desire.

#### Conclusion: 
In *Juste la fin du monde*, Xavier Dolan explores the impossibility of genuine communication within the constraints of familial and social structures. From a Lacanian perspective, the film delves into the limits of language, the pain of unfulfilled desire, and the alienation that arises when one's identity is trapped within the gaze of the Other. Louis’s tragic silence and the family’s emotional turmoil highlight the struggle between the Real, the Imaginary, and the Symbolic, creating a poignant exploration of isolation and existential pain.

## Mulheres transexuais em Almodovar

Pedro Almodóvar is known for his deep engagement with LGBTQ+ themes, including his nuanced portrayal of trans women in several of his films. His work often explores themes of identity, transformation, and the challenges faced by trans individuals within society. Below are key examples of trans women in Almodóvar's films:
1. **
Pedro Almodóvar is known for his deep engagement with LGBTQ+ themes, including his nuanced portrayal of trans women in several of his films. His work often explores themes of identity, transformation, and the challenges faced by trans individuals within society. Below are key examples of trans women in Almodóvar's films:
1. Agrado in *All About My Mother* (1999)** :
Agrado, played by Antonia San Juan, is one of Almodóvar’s most iconic trans characters. She is a trans woman sex worker with a strong, unapologetic personality. Agrado brings a combination of humor, warmth, and resilience to the film. Her famous monologue about the surgeries and procedures she’s undergone to become more "authentic" is both a critique and celebration of self-construction, a core theme in Almodóvar’s films. This speech not only reflects Agrado’s personal journey but also touches on the universal desire for self-acceptance and identity construction.

Agrado, played by Antonia San Juan, is one of Almodóvar’s most iconic trans characters. She is a trans woman sex worker with a strong, unapologetic personality. Agrado brings a combination of humor, warmth, and resilience to the film. Her famous monologue about the surgeries and procedures she’s undergone to become more "authentic" is both a critique and celebration of self-construction, a core theme in Almodóvar’s films. This speech not only reflects Agrado’s personal journey but also touches on the universal desire for self-acceptance and identity construction.
2. **
Pedro Almodóvar is known for his deep engagement with LGBTQ+ themes, including his nuanced portrayal of trans women in several of his films. His work often explores themes of identity, transformation, and the challenges faced by trans individuals within society. Below are key examples of trans women in Almodóvar's films:
